![](images/45dbb4e118bb1feac42c459eb8cb635e8df3561a9f22c8f735a55aa6c19bc828.jpg)

# hong kong

a history of manhua Wendy Siuyi Wong

Enter the world of manhua and enter an exotic world of political satire, kung-fu fighters, and sexy teenage animé. With more than 1,000 illustrations, Hong Kong Comics is the first overview of the colorful, immediate, and always-captivating universe of Chinese manhua and Japanese manga—broad terms that include cartoons, comics, and picture story books. The growing interest in these fascinating illustrations comes both from a desire for increasingly exotic forms of entertainment and from an ever-increasing curiosity about the artistic and cultural traditions of their originating countries. In this lavishly illustrated tome, Wendy Siuyi Wong traces the art form of manhua from its beginnings in the early twentieth century to its most influential contemporary practitioners and, in the process, reveals the development of a unique Hong Kong style. She examines a wide variety of comics (each with an English annotation), from satirical political cartoons to lighthearted funny papers, from children's stories to action-filled martial art works, bringing to the reader—whether illustrator or comic book fan—an encyclopedic look at this undiscovered and rich world of graphic art.

![](images/b60e80c6c5098c9358735c450b64eb7639228ef64d851516926454c589920be8.jpg)

![](images/fd4e153f84b1c294bcec9f6fbf8af72dfdfb2572894b68718a44ef0ad353135c.jpg)

![](images/ab4bfe3f61e7547c8eab9362147b9e71cac5156e45e1444ad4d7670a45379e41.jpg)

## hong kong comics:香港 history of manhua 汽漫

Wendy Siuyi Wong 画

Princeton Architectural Press

New York

![](images/8d5fff88623cf58c3039b1fa6a75a9b823e89668517ae9dd8cb8285953617f34.jpg)

## contents

preface p.7   
introduction p.11   
part 1: the paper tiger—satirical & political manhua p.26   
part 2: the art of humor—comical manhua p.50   
part 3: spirits of kung fu—action manhua p.98   
part 4: borrowed tales—children's manhua p.132   
appendices p.168   
reference sources   
selected artist biographies   
translation conversion index

![](images/eade62a020267a897b81a8481c48d98276e2e61bcbe171ec841767bd770c5cea.jpg)

After the Second World War, comic books became one of the most popular reading materials in Hong Kong. Comic books were considered low-brow entertainment for the enjoyment of the working class and children, and its art was thought to be insignificant. However, as comic books increased in popularity and commercial value, they became an important money-making industry. Although the Hong Kong comics market is primarily influenced by Japanese manga and animation, it has developed into its own, individual style. Hong Kong comics has a distinct drawing style, storytelling format, and cultural identity.

There are more than forty locally produced titles (excluding Japanese comics) published on a regular basis and sold to the six million people of Hong Kong. Manhua, the local term for “comics," comes in many forms: cartoons, caricatures, political and editorial satire, comics, graphic novels, and lianhuantu (Chinese-style action comics). It reflects the social and cultural changes of Hong Kong life and is an expression of Hong Kong identity. This book, the first of its kind on Chinese comics, aims to facilitate Western culture's understanding of contemporary Chinese popular culture in Hong Kong. Most of the images displayed have never been published in the West, in presenting them here, the hope is to promote Hong Kong comics to the English-speaking world.

The history of Chinese conic arts has not yet been fully explored, and if the visual language of comic arts, cartoons, illustration, and animation is universal, this book enhances the complete history of these art forms. Throughout the comics found in this book, an intermingling of influences— European, American, Japanese—combine with Chinese heritage to produce the Hong Kong-style manhua. Although Hong Kong manhua preserved a unique cultural identity, readers may find visual images that are familiar. Yet they will also be introduced to subjects that are not found in the Western social and cultural setting.

This book was originally published in Chinese for a Hong Kong audience. Funding was provided by the Hong Kong Arts Development Council. This version contains three sections: Introduction, Annotated Captions, and Appendices. The introductory essay is a survey of the development of Hong Kong comics through the decades and includes a brief background of the history of Chinese comics. Four genres are represented in the annotated captions: satirical and political manhua, comical manhua, action manhua, and children's manhua. They include approximately

800 illustrations from Hong Kong as well as titles taken from the history of Chinese comics. The appendices include selected artist biographies, reference sources, and a translation conversion index.

Because no definitive work on Hong Kong comics has been done in English, the title translations are primarily the author's but include the established version given by the existing title. The Chinese name convention, in which the family name precedes the given name, is used throughout the book based on the romanization of Cantonese, the Chinese dialect spoken in Hong Kong. The English names of the artists are used when they have already been established. The translation conversion index at the end of the book provides a reference for readers with knowledge of both English and Chinese.

This book owes a great deal to the coauthor of the original Chinese version, Yueng Wai-pong. As the collector of Hong Kong and Chinese comics, Yueng has spent his lifetime collecting and studying comics. With his kind permission, this book could be rewritten and published in English. Without Yueng, and all the Hong Kong comic artists and friends who assisted us in the original Chinese version, this worldwide English version could never have been undertaken. I would also like to acknowledge Ellen Lupton, who introduced the Chinese version to Princeton Architectural Press. At Princeton Architectural Press, special thanks to Jennifer Thompson, Kevin Lippert, and Mark Lamster. Thanks also to the university where I teach, SUNY Institute of Technology at Utica, Eileen Kenneally, Susan Goodwin, Cynthia Lee, Joyce and Harlan Cuklanz, Carina Wong, Jenny Wong, Jackie Kwok and Michael Siu at the Hong Kong Polytechnic University, and my family for their encouragement. Finally, but not least, I wholeheartedly thank Lisa Cuklanz, who provided significant contributions on the initial draft, understanding, and much more. This book is for Kyle on his first birthday and is dedicated to Hong Kong manhua.

Wendy Siuyi Wong October 2001

![](images/c589d18617e51847d114c8e0b9ad3ddd3229e441f167df566c80687205a02603.jpg)

# introduction: a history of hong kong comics

The Chinese word manhua is the equivalent of “comics” or “cartoons"” in English; its Chinese character is the same as that of manga, the Japanese term for “comics." Although it’s difficult to trace the exact origin of the term, most historians of Chinese comics believe the contemporary usage of manhua was borrowed from Japan at the beginning of the twentieth century. Today, manhua means all the forms and styles of cartoons, comics, and lianhuantu (a traditional illustrated storybook) in Hong Kong. In tracing the development of the general term manhua, more specific terms are sometimes used to refer to smaller subgenres. Car-ton, as direct translation of the word “cartoon," for example, refers to political satire and caricature as well as more lighthearted works depicting everyday life. In this book, however, manhua refers to all Hong Kong cartoons, caricatures, political and editorial satire, comics, graphic novels, and lianhuantu.

In Hong Kong, as in most East Asian societies, comics are one of the most popular things to read. Although the city's population is only about 6.7 million, more than forty titles are regularly published there. The market is divided into two parts: manhua produced by local artists and Chinese versions of Japanese manga (which dominate the newsstands). Even though manga are currently more popular, only local manhua reflect the cultural values and details of everyday life in Hong Kong. Over the past century, Hong Kong manhua have evolved parallel to the changing and sometimes turbulent social and political environments of mainland China and Hong Kong.

## The Beginning: 1867–1927

Chinese drawings similar to modern cartoons existed in ancient times and appear throughout the country's history. The oldest surviving examples are stone reliefs from the eleventh century B.C. and pottery from 5000 to 3000 B.c. Other examples include symbolic brush drawings from the Ming Dynasty (A.D. 1368–1644), a satirical drawing titled Peacocks by the early Qing Dynasty (1643– 1911) artist Zhu Da, and a work called Ghosts’ Farce Pictures from around 1771, by Luo Liang-feng.

Another ancestor of the modern Hong Kong comic is nian hua, the traditional New Year prints made during the Ming and Qing Dynasties. These mass-produced, colored wood-block prints were important decorations for the New Year and were hung in houses for good luck. Many nian hua were humorous or satirical, though their satire was seldom political. An exception is some of the

![](images/2aedb4d3b53e87a5196375b01e05512cd23344a8e1b5d3818a991aba3a77b5b8.jpg)  
The True Face of the Nation's Betrayer in the Present, artist unknown, late Qing dynasty

prints distributed during the late nineteenth century, which contained xenophobic messages.

The development of modern Chinese cartoons began with the introduction of Western lithographic printing methods at the end of the nineteenth century. This new technology not only boosted the circulation of newspapers and magazines, but it also allowed for the printing of cartoons, even in color. Beginning in about 1875, caricatures and satirical drawings appeared in numerous periodicals as complements to their editorial text. The nascent art forms were then known by several names: fengci hua (satirical drawing), yuyi hua (allegorical picture), zhengzhi hua (political picture), shishi hua (current pic-

ture), baodao hua (reporting picture), jilu hua (recording picture), huaji hua (amusement picture), and xiao hua (funnies).

Another predecessor of the Chinese comic is lianhuantu, a palm-size picture book of sequential drawings. This format, which has existed for centuries, consisted of a series of pictures with brief captions above them. Their purpose was to tell stories in a way that was accessible to illiterate or marginally literate readers and typically depicted traditional Chinese stories. It entered the Hong Kong market in the 1920s. Usually imported from Shanghai, they could be found at street kiosks, where they were rented for a small fee. These rental kiosks were a common sight even during the Japanese occupation, and they didn't completely disappear until the 1970s. As with modern comic books, lianhuantu were considered an unschooled medium, meant for a mass audience with little education or aesthetic sophistication.

Chinese manhua was born in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, roughly during the years 1867 to 1927. Early manhua aimed at satire and caricature, and though they were influenced by traditional drawing styles, they were essentially a new art form introduced by foreigners. Hong Kong's first magazine of satirical cartoons, The China Punch, was founded by a British journalist in 1867. It was based on the British satirical journal The Punch and retained the style of political caricature, illustration, and humor of its model. Although published in Hong Kong and concerned with culture and events in China, it was written in English, because its target audience was primarily foreigners living there. The China Punch had a heavy colonial flavor, it made fun of local customs and in general upheld the assumed superiority of British norms and values. Notwithstanding its Western bias, The China Punch is significant beacause it introduced Hong Kong to humorous and satirical cartoons as a genre.

The first cartoon drawn by a Chinese person in Hong Kong was Tse Tsan-tai's The Situation in the Far East, which was printed in Japan in 1899. Born and raised in Australia, Tse moved to Hong Kong in 1887, at the age of 17. He soon became a supporter and collaborator of the revolutionary Sun Yat-Sen, and in 1903 he cofounded the South China Morning Post, now the oldest English language newspaper in Hong Kong. Tse and others were upset about the foreign enclaves and“treaty ports” that had been established in the decades following the First Opium War by way of often unfair agreements signed by the Qing government.

# 圖局時

![](images/96c7d92b1a0730a4b05644e0cf416acfb6fb2c2ac6d893af58db3d398e107e06.jpg)  
The Situation in the Far East, by Tse Tsan-tai, 1899

Tse's goal with his cartoon was to educate his countrymen about the aggressive ambitions of foreign powers in China and to caution them about their destructive intentions. It used animals and other symbols to represent foreign countries and their interests. The cartoon was widely reprinted in mainland China and overseas without crediting Tse. Because of this unauthorized and uncredited distribution, Chinese historians often credit Tse's cartoon to a Shanghai cartoonist.

Political cartoons were one of the most accessible ways to express opposition to the Qing government; caricature was particularly effective in depicting its weakness and corruption. Due to its unique position—detached from both the mainland and the government—Hong Kong served as an outlet and an important base of support for early political cartoonists. From 1895 until the revolution in 1911, the territory was home to revolutionary newspapers that encouraged the overthrow of the Qing government and the establishment of a new Republic of China. And when the anti-Qing The Journal of Current Pictorial was banned on the mainland in 1905, its cartoonists moved to Hong Kong and published ten more issues before the colonial government ceded to Qing pressure and shut it down.

Before Sun Yat-Sen established the Republic of China in 1911, his revolutionary group, the Chinese Alliance, often used Hong Kong to produce and circulate anti-Qing materials. In 1907, they circulated a supplement to their newspaper, the People’'s Journal, called Condemnations from Heaven. A cartoon in the supplement titled The True Face of the Nation’s Betrayer in the Present, depicted high-ranking officers of the Qing government with their heads chopped off or split open. Not surprisingly, this triggered a strong reaction by Qing officials, who protested to Hong Kong's colonial government.Hong Kong quickly established a new regulation to stop the printing and distribution of these revolutionary cartoons.

![](images/449501e317acac40073c6f442c0df6b89d4853f364b49cd7bb68665d3d31482e.jpg)

![](images/3fe58f84e0e66f30ce97c4ef00abd5cdcb028c2f7e9f17090e7497804fdb88ea.jpg)  
The True Face of the Nation's Betrayer in the Present, artist unknown, late Qing dynasty

In 1912, He Jian-shi, Zheng Nu-quan, and the other

artists who had published The Journal ofCurrent Pictorial began a new publication in Shanghai, The True Record. This magazine criticized the government of the new Republic of China, especially the political struggles within the Guomindang (GMD, or Nationalist Party). The True Record was banned in 1913, and some of its artists, including Zheng Nu-quan, fled to Hong Kong. The owner of Hong Kong's Leung Kwok-ying Drugstore admired the social power of cartoons and supported Zheng to continue his work. However, due to illness, the cartoonist did not live to see the publication of Renjian Pictorial in 1920, a book of approximately 190 cartoons criticizing the politics and culture of mainland China.

The True Record and Renjian Pictorial are typical of early manhua. The genre matured in the shadow of the revolutionary struggle in China and was closely connected to politics and war. While cartoons gradually became less political and more mainstream in the 1920s, they were still a popular

medium for promoting political messages both in Hong Kong and in mainland China.

The foundation for the development of manhua as an art form rather than a political venue was laid in the 1920s. The word manhua” itself became more widely used—an important linguistic shift——after the famous writer Feng Zi-kai published his first collection of cartoons, Zi-kai Manhua, in 1925. Feng’s fame drew attention to his use of the word; the phrase “Zi-kai Manhua” was soon commonly used, associating the artist's style with Japanese manga. Although the term manhua had existed before, the publication of Feng’s collection led it to take precedence over the many other descriptions of cartoon art. Under this more unified term, cartoons

Zi-kai Manhua, by Feng Zi-kai, 1925

began to attain the status of a specialized art form, rather than being the loose collection of styles of previous decades.

## Emerging: 1927–1949

Prior to about 1927, most manhua focused on revolutionary politics and current events. By the 1930s, though, their content, style, and manner of expression began to change and improve. Chinese cartoonists became aware of the three essential ingredients of manhua—a powerful idea, the representation of people or events, and a thought-provoking style of narrative—and their work was soon recognized as a powerful art form, attracting the praise and involvement of famous artists, such as the writer Lu Xun. While Shanghai remained the center of manhua production, comics' popularity began to spread to other cities, including Guangzhou, Tianjin, and Hong Kong.

Before the Second World War, Hong Kong manhua followed the stylistic developments of mainland comics, particularly those drawn in Shanghai and Guangzhou. With the establishment in 1927 of the Cartoon Association (Manhua Hui), the first such trade organization in China, the activities of cartoonists became more organized. The association helped to solidify the loosely organized network of artists that made up the comics industry, and it encouraged efforts to raise the quality of its products.

The first Chinese cartoon magazine, Shanghai Sketch, appeared in 1928. The eight-page, tabloid-size journal was printed with two-color lithography and included four pages of manhua, by such artists as Ye Qian-yu, Zhang Guang-yu, and Huang Wen-nong. The first serial comics with regular characters—heavily influenced by Western serials such as Gilbert Dalziel's Ally Sloper's Half

Holiday and Richard Outcault's The Yellow Kid—was Ye Qian-yu's Mr. Wang, who appeared in Shanghai Sketch in 1928. Numerous imitators sprang up to compete with Shanghai Sketch, first in Shanghai and then south into the Guangzhou area.

Between 1934 and 1937, about seventeen cartoon magazines were published in Shanghai. Modern Sketch, first published in 1934, was the most important after Shanghai Sketch. It included many outstanding artists of the time and established a standard for emerging art. Most of the cartoons produced during these years were political and satirical cartoons, often anti-Japanese propaganda.

Beginning in 1934, many newspapers added sections devoted to manhua. This new outlet for Chinese cartoons was put to propaganda use in 1937, as Japan threatened and then launched war. For the next eight years, almost all manhua focused on the war, which China was losing. When the Japanese occupied Shanghai, many antiwar cartoon magazines relocated to Guangzhou, until it too was occupied in 1939. After that, Hong Kong became the headquarters for the manhua industry, and many of the cartoonists oved to the British colony. In addition to publishing antiwar manhua, Hong Kong hosted an antiwar cartoon exhibition in 1939, The Modern Chinese Cartoonist Exhibition, organized by Ye Qian-yu. All manhua activity stopped in 1941, when Hong Kong was also captured by Japan

![](images/3897d282f7de1772e4e2042dbb847497abe32dafa6422fe3084e3ea60d09fe95.jpg)  
Mr. Wang, by Ye Qian-yu, 1928

![](images/1ecfc201840236f0d8fa4ab7d7407022a5806b404adc3a4b8fc516b37514cb6d.jpg)

With the defeat of Japan in 1945, on both the Chinese and Allied fronts, political cartoons rapidly diminished, and the content of manhua shifted to serial stories about everyday life. However, the continued instability of mainland China kept alive some political

The Modern Chinese Cartoonist Exhibition, brochure cover, artist unknown, 1939

cartoons that documented the struggles between the Nationalists and the Communists. The most significant work of this period was This Is a Cartoon Era, published in 1947 by the anti-Nationalist manhua artists group Renjian Huahui, which included mainland cartoonists who had been purged by the GMD and fled to Hong Kong. After the Communists seized power in 1949, most pro-Communist artists returned to the mainland.

## Rising: 1949–1968

After the physical damage of the war with Japan—and given the ongoing political damage of the civil war on the mainland—Hong Kong was eager for redevelopment and a return to normalcy. The reappearance of newspaper comic strips was one of the first signs of this return. These four-panel strips, which focused on daily life rather than on politics, became the main format for Hong Kong manhua for the next thirty years. They were a decidedly popular medium, with dialogue written in colloquial Cantonese rather than in literary Chinese.

Newspapers were the main venue for manhua between 1945 and 1949; the ongoing civil war curtailed the market for stand-alone comic books. Yuan Bou-wan's Kiddy Cheung, Miss Lau, and Wong Chai, which represented the lower classes, were very popular newspaper strips. In 1950, Kiddy Cheung was made into a movie featuring the six-year-old Bruce Lee. Yuan's strips were later collected into best-selling comic books.

Hong Kong began to flourish in the 1950s as money and people fleeing the Communist mainland poured in. Demand for cultural goods exploded, and the variety of publications steadily increased. Before the revolution, the mainland had been the primary source for Chinese-language publications. After 1949, however, non-Communist subject matter was considered evil, and Hong Kong became the only place in China with the experience and talent needed to produce manhua for commercial purposes. With the end of the civil war, the rebirth of the Hong Kong market, and the increasing demand from Chinese-speaking markets in Southeast Asia and North America, manhua production in Hong Kong expanded rapidly.

Cut off from the popular culture of mainland China, Hong Kong's cartoonists turned to American comics for inspiration and gradually evolved a unique hybrid of Eastern and Western styles. American comic books had been available before the war, but because of their language and cultural differences, as well as their high price, they were never very popular.

Hong Kong, like America, experienced a baby boom after 1945, which created a ready market for children's manhua. By the early 1950s, many American kids' comic books had been translated and published in Hong Kong, popularizing such characters as Pinnochio, Mickey Mouse, Donald Duck, and Elmer Fudd. Many locally produced comics carried obvious American influences too. Little Angeli, created by the Bao brothers in 1954, featured small, round-faced characters modeled after Little Abner, Popeye, and other American examples.

But Hong Kong manhua also had a style of their own. The characters had exaggerated features and Chinese clothes; the story lines dealt with Chinese themes or events; and there were more panels per page than in American comics. The longest-running children's magazine was Children's

Paradise, first published in 1953. Created and edited by Law Guan-tsiu, a Guangzhou artist who came to Hong Kong shortly after the war, the manhua also became popular among Chinese outside Hong Kong, particularly in Southeast Asia. More than 1,000 issues of Children's Paradise were produced before the title was finally retired in 1995.

The era's most important and influential manhua magazine for adults was Cartoons World, first published in 1956. It published work by established cartoonists but also nurtured a new generation of manhua artists, who eventually provided the talent for the comics boom of the 1960s. As with children's manhua, American comics had a big influence on the developing Hong Kong style. In Hui Guan-man's best-selling Uncle

![](images/c2da2ff0db66f740223eee0dfe006a5d41d5e7d27efb0a7d82370ae05e8326be.jpg)  
Children's Paradise, edited by Law Guan-tsiu, 1953

![](images/dc124e02a890171e2995dc7c9bc21041b8630ae58011376ced3b4914bfe00f7e.jpg)  
Uncle Choi, by Hui Guan-man, 1958

Choi—which initially described the life of a simple-minded soldier but quickly transformed into a more serious wartime narrative——American comics elements were integrated with traditional Chinese line-art drawings. As a result, more panels could be fit on a single page, better story lines created, and more sophisticated characters developed.

Uncle Choi was a huge success, and some of its editorial and formal innovations proved very influential. Unlike most manhua of the time, for instance, Uncle Choi presented characters in modern clothes, and its humor was contemporary. It also came in a different size, five by seven inches, rather than the then-traditional 3.6 by 5 inches. Other artists began using the larger format, and soon a new periodical, Caricature Weekly, switched to the even greater size of a tabloid newspaper. This format, two sheets of eight unbound pages,

sold for ten cents and was referred to as a penny paper.

The inexpensive journal was so successful that it soon became a daily; it also spawned many imitators. Some of its notable progeny were Entertainment News, New Comics Weekly, Epoch Comics Weekly, Daily Comics, The Cartoon and Comic Gardens, Radar Pictorial, and Companion Comics. The main attractions of these tabloid-format manhua papers were cartoon strips and short pictorials created by local comics artists. American cartoons and comics, reprinted without permission, were also included. Readers of these manhua were not always interested in quality, and most of the papers were poorly produced; but the penny papers were still a great success, and they contributed to the rapid expansion of the Hong Kong manhua industry in the early to mid-1960s. The tabloid format itself began to disappear in the late 1960s.

By the middle of the decade, more readers were interested in comic books with a single, ongoing narrative. The best-sellers among these serial manhua were Old Master Q, by Wong Chak, Dumb Detective, by Song Sam-long, and 13-Dot Cartoons, by Lee Wai-chun. Each of these manhua characters represented a different social stereotype, and each had its own audience. Only Old Master Q is still published today; it is the longest-running Chinese manhua serial.

From the mid-1950s to the mid-1960s, publishers and artists tended to avoid controversial subjects. Most manhua were based on everyday life in Hong Kong, with only the occasional criticism of social problems such as housing, inflation, education, and crime. The late 1960s, the height of the Cultural Revolution in mainland China, saw some exceptions to this general avoidance of political subjects. Editorial pages in both pro- and anti-Communist newspapers published cartoons criticizing the opposition. Pro-Communist cartoons focused on the evils of imperialism and criticized Hong Kong's colonial government. Even children's manhua in pro-Communist newspapers were full of propagandistic fervor.

One of the most prominent anti-Communist cartoonists was E-king Yen. His early work in Dai Jung Daily and Express Daily dealt with international politics and the Cultural Revolution-era power struggles on the mainland. He also strongly criticized Communist activity in Hong Kong, especially the riots of 1967, and other Hong Kong artists began to direct their attention toward these events. Even so, political cartoons never again played as prominent a role as they did before the war. After the Cultural Revolution ended in 1976, most people lost interest in politics, and ideological comics receded into near invisibility.

In the 1960s, the manhua industry was challenged by the widespread distribution of pirated comics. Bootleg Japanese manga and Taiwanese comics were a bargain compared to Hong Kong manhua since the same ten cents bought more pages in the unauthorized versions. The industry's response was to change the standard size of manhua to five by seven inches, increase the number of pages, and boost the price to twenty cents. Some Hong Kong manhua were published in books as large as seven by ten inches. Five by seven inches became—and still is——the most common size for locally produced manhua. The effort might have helped local comics compete with pirated foreign books, but it did little against a more serious adversary. Television was introduced to Hong Kong in 1967, and manhua would never regain its peak level of popularity.

## Establishing: 1969–1989

Television proved to be a particularly attractive medium in Hong Kong because it introduced citizens

to foreign popular cultures, especially that of Japan and the United States. As this new competition for leisure time grew, Hong Kong manhua were forced to move in new directions.

The most popular and lucrative of these new directions was the kung fu manhua, modeled after the martial arts movies, especially Bruce Lee’s films, that dominated the Hong Kong box office in the early 1970s. Kung fu fever let the masses escape the unpleasant aspects of their daily lives. Comics based on the same story lines and action became immensely popular, and they remained one of the dominant strains of manhua throughout the 1970s and 1980s.

![](images/3ea3bd4dc92e95e89b7941730b240f725057110a35f66f54db4a0b25247ad3ac.jpg)  
Little Rascals (Siu Lau-man), by Wong Yuk-long, 1970

![](images/e5b2f488594e6a4bd2e7eb93f163eb0af7a1007e32b885c422f6b9709716ef0c.jpg)  
Children reading lianhuantu at a temporary street kiosk renting manhua, 1950s

The visual style and drawing techniques of kung fu comics were veró much influenced by American action comics and Japanese manga of the 1950s and 1960s. Yet this new genre also combined illustrations and storytelling in a manner quite similar to the traditional lianhuantu format. Among the most successful artists to workon kung fu comics was Wong Yuk-long (Tony Wong), who combined Japanese techniques and characters with his knowledge of traditional lianhuantu. His first successful comics were Little Vagabond (1968), Son of Ultra-Man (1969), and Solar Lord (1969), but his biggest hit was Little Rascals (Siu Lau-man) (1970), a serial lianhuantu about a group of young people living in a Hong Kong public housing project. These good guys used violent means to enforce justice against gangsters and other dubious characters. Not only was Wong's comic a best-seller, so

too were its many imitators, the best of which was Seung-gun Siu-bo's Lee Siu-lung (Bruce Lee's Chinese name).

The explicit violence of kung fu manhua may have helped sell comic books, but it also attracted the attention of the Hong Kong government. In 1975, a new Indecent Publications Law took effect, which limited the scope and types of violence and characters allowed in manhua magazines. Little Rascals (the literal translation of the title is actually “little gangsters") was renamed Oriental Heroes (Lung Fu Mun). The new name suggested strength and fortitude rather than antisocial, underworld behavior. The story line too was changed: the characters no longer lived in Hong Kong's public housing projects, but were instead usually based in Japan. Plots were less realistic and more clearly fictional, thus avoiding the criticism that they acted as negative role models for young readers or reflected a negative image of Hong Kong.

The law applied only to magazines and comic books; daily newspapers were spared the censorship. This created a big loophole, which was exploited by artists such as Wong and Seung-gun, who changed the format of their comics from a magazine format to a daily tabloid newspaper. They did, however, make the violence in their manhua less graphic. Even with this slight self-censorship, Wong's and Seung-gun's work soon dominated the Hong Kong manhua market, and their story lines and drawing styles remained fairly constant for years.

A breakthrough in drawing style finally occurred in 1982, in Ma Wing-shing's The Chinese Hero (Chung-wah Ying-hung), first published as a supplement to Wong's other major kung fu title,

Drunken Master. Ma's innovative, realistic drawings utilized bold, solid colors and detailed faces resembling real people. The style was heavily influenced and inspired by Japanese manga artist Ryoichi Ikegami. The Chinese Hero was an immediate success when it then appeared separately from Drunken Master, making Ma, who had received little formal education, the protagonist of a rags-toriches story. The fame he received helped change the perception of manhua drawing, legitimizing it as an art form.

While kung fu comics came to dominate the Hong Kong market, they were just one of several new directions manhua took in the early 1970s. Some experimental publications appeared, although they were inevitably short-lived. Experimental Cartoons and Cartoons News, for example, were published in 1975 by a group of young artists and edited by Yuan Yat-mu, Mok Wah, and Yeung Waipong. These two magazines were the first to introduce manhua criticism to Hong Kong. They also encouraged a serious attitude toward making manhua and introduced new concepts and forms from foreign comics. Unfortunately, both magazines managed to put out just three issues before their meager budgets were depleted. Yet despite the magazines' failures, the manhua artists who created them continued to organize manhua projects outside the mainstream.

As Hong Kong comics developed, certain genres, such as social, satirical, and political manhua, were often overlooked. Not until the 1980s, when the handover of Hong Kong from Britain to China began to loom, did political cartoons become an active genre again. The most prominent artists of these new political manhua were Zunzi and Ma Long, both of whom were established editorial cartoonists for daily newspapers. Their work focused primarily on British and Chinese

policies in Hong Kong and the incompetence of the Hong Kong government. In addition to their daily work in newspapers and magazines, they regularly published their manhua collections in book form, such as Zunzi's Grandpa Deng (1994 and 1997) and Ma’s Political Comics Collection (1990). These political manhua were considered “alternative” because the mainstream market was dominated by action, martial arts, and manga-inspired comics.

## Exploring: 1990 to the Present

Hong Kong experienced a bloom of cultural diversity during the years before the handover to China, a bloom that extended to manhua. While the mainstream market was divided between manga and locally produced comics that involved gambling, romance, ghosts, and sword fighting, a few artists were experimenting with new drawing techniques, more complex story lines, and more diverse subject matter. Different types of work gradually emerged, though it was difficult for this material to find a niche within previously existing genres.

![](images/aeb0eea7603647b7e2abb9a5f1756de7e1854425a292bcc13ac0da6591877b1d.jpg)  
Editorial cartoon by Zunzi, 1993

In the early 1990s, “three-part stories," which were violent comics set in Hong Kong, became increasingly popular. These manhua—such as Teddy Boy, Portland Street, and Red Light District, all published in 1992——depicted violence in a new way, with realistic locations and situations that suggested their events were actually happening (or at least could happen) in Hong Kong. In response, the government amended the indecent publications law by restricting some of these new manhua to adult readers and making the publishers put them in a sealed wrapper with a warning message attached.

![](images/e742642372552b328f665d24196c954496149c5e45292dde36a845713b7e5dd5.jpg)  
Cockroach Tenicolor Comix Quarterly, spring issue, 1999

Other artists continued to work on political cartoons, children's comics, and experimental titles. The most popular kids’ comic was Yellow Bus (1996), drawn by Mak Ga-bik (Alice Mak), with script by Tse Lap-man, and featuring a pig named McMug. The drawing style and story line appealed to both adults and children, and as had happened with other “cute” characters in Japan, Mcmug products, such as stuffed toys, stationery, and pens and pencils, greatly extended the title's popularity.

The trend toward alternative manhua for young adults began in the late 1980s, when the artist Li Chi-tat began to create highly detailed manhua heavily influenced by Japanese manga. He and other artists, including Au-Yeung Craig and Lai Tat-wing, appealed to audiences looking for a more sophisticated style. Au-Yeung, who has a degree in graphic design, approached manhua as a combination of illustration and art, and created story lines that are at times fragmented. Lai Tatwing also had a distinctive drawing style, which is sometimes an experiment with different compositions, points of view, and new ways of using dialogue (he even created whole works without any dialogue). The success of these artists illustrated the possibilities for self-expression and artistic exploration within the medium and provided a model for a new generation of artists.

![](images/86af2931339798fb7fccede800fbae02355182b509f8f27c947e1251d27c7ce6.jpg)  
Mom's Drawer Is at the Bottom, by Lau Lee-lee, 1998

One of these younger artists, Lau Lee-lee (Lily Lau), is the first self-proclaimed feminist manhua artist. Her first comic, Mom's Drawer Is at the Bottom, was published in 1998. Though her work, infused with feminist themes, such as a critique of gender inequality and heterosexual relationships, might seem radical for the general public, it was met with a positive reception. Her drawing style is graphic, and she did not hesitate to depict women's and men's bodies in sexually explicit scenes.

Recent work by young, alternative manhua artists has

brought new vigor to the industry. Still, support for manhua as part of Hong Kong culture— especially for preserving its history and promoting the understanding of its cultural importance—is uncommon. A few prominent artists, such as Au-Yeung Craig and Lau Lee-lee, have received funding from the Hong Kong Arts Development Council, but that organization's focus is more on the production of comics than the analysis of them. In 1998 the Council did sponsor Cartoonet, a critical publication that explored the importance of manhua in Hong Kong's popular culture, but the project only lasted for two issues.

Prior to Cartoonet, some short-lived manhua reviews were published. Monthly Comic Magazine published five issues in 1990, then ceased, and published three more issues in 1993. It was the only record of the industry in the mid-1990s, and while it is a partial record, it is an improvement on previous decades, for which no overall record exists at all. Notwithstanding critical shortcomings, Hong Kong manhua have thrived creatively over the past decade. Though begun as a satellite to the mainland comics industry—an unstable collection of titles rented from temporary street kiosksthe Hong Kong manhua business has become strong and independent, able to withstand successive shocks of foreign influences. Now that Hong Kong is once again under Chinese rule, the industry may face more competition. On the other hand, the new relationship with mainland China offers the chance for Hong Kong manhua to reach a much larger audience. So while it is still unclear how reunification with China will affect Hong Kong manhua, judging from the events of the past century, it seems likely that new innovations will sustain the industry and encourage ever changing forms of comic art. Hong Kong manhua will continue to evolve, changing in accordance with economic, political, and cultural shifts.

![](images/6c5d9d1b614fff577b3c6122a454b25f7f1ce9b0b4b1b03175187650a98973d0.jpg)

![](images/5ae5a8234eb82052895dac407e2437face97f89f6074d4ddb3ba04d2e0e56b64.jpg)

Satirical and political manhua were never considered mainstream reading under British colonial rule. The China Punch, a magazine begun by British journalists in 1867, was one of the first venues for political cartoons in Chinese territory, but it was aimed at English readers, and its content often denigrated Chinese customsand practices. The first truly Chinese political cartoon didn't appear in Hong Kong until 1899, when Tse Tsan-tai published The Situation in the Far East, which illustrated Western threats to China. Since then, and despite the fact that they were never popular reading, cartoons have been used to express critical views of Chinese government and society.

In the early decades of the twentieth century, politically active artists opposed to the Qing imperial government used cartoons to spread revolutionary messages. Leading illustrators—among them Pan Da-wei, Gao Jian-fu, Xie Ying-bo, He Jian-shi, and Zheng Nu-quan—began to publish this kind of work in The Journal of Current Pictorials (1905) and The True Record (1912). After the establishment of the Republic of China in 1911, the same artists used comics to critique the new government. When the Republic banned The True Record in 1913, Zheng Nu-quan fled to Hong Kong. The comics he drew there were printed posthumously in 1920 in Renjian Pictorial, then considered one of the few important political comics produced in Hong Kong before the Second World War.

Shanghai, not Hong Kong, was the center of satirical and political cartooning before the war. The periodicals produced there during the 1930s, such as Shanghai Sketch (1928), Modern Sketch (1934), and Shanghai Manhua (1936), are the era's classic examples of political and social manhua. However, after Japan invaded China in 1937—eventually occupying Shanghai—Hong Kong became an important source of comics, especially those with an anti-Japanese or antiwar agenda.

During the Chinese civil war of the late 1940s, many cartoons with anti-Nationalist Party, pro-Communist messages were published in Hong Kong, notably in the journal This Is a Cartoon Era. After the Communists established the People's Republic, relations between the mainland and the British colony were strained, but refugees fleeing Communist rule came to Hong Kong looking for a new life, not continued political activity. Political cartoons quickly declined in importance.

In the decades following the revolution, Communist propaganda comics produced on the mainland were readily available in Hong Kong. However, these ideological manhua were never popular. On the other side of the political spectrum, anti-Communist messages were found in such Hong Kong manhua as Three Men Going Home (1955) and The World of E-king Yen (1968), which were often sold alongside the pro-Communist titles.

From 1960 through the late 1980s, political comics were an insignificant part of the Hong Kong market, which was dominated by humor and kung fu manhua. As the handover of Hong Kong to China grew closer, political cartoons came back into style. After the protests on the mainland in 1989——and the massacre at Tiananmen Square on June 4—people in Hong Kong became more politically aware and active. During this period, editorial cartoonists for the daily newspapers, including Zunzi, Ma Lung, Local Boy, Yuan Yat-muk, and Apink, recorded and commented on political events in Hong Kong. Zunzi, one of the most productive artists of the era, was particularly successful with political caricature. He drew familiar figures, including top Chinese and Hong Kong officials, as well as fictional figures representative of Hong Kong politicians. Many other artists produced satirical and political comics, capturing this important moment in Hong Kong's history.

After Hong Kong was handed back to China, artists produced political manhua that criticized the Special Administrative Region (SAR) government and even mainland China's top officials. Although political criticism was not banned by the new government, the work of many artists reflected some degree of self-censorship. More damaging, though, has been the decrease in the number of outlets for political and satirical cartoons since the handover in July, 1997.

![](images/ec41c29814e905aa50363e79229cd6d34f2bf12f96b7b2334271c555aab229e9.jpg)

![](images/aa1574b33c5b81cb52e373c182e5de304b05923650b8e738720b3843262852cd.jpg)

## 1–4.

The China Punch, 1867

Art/Script: Unknown (British)

Humor, satire, and other elements of Western caricature were introduced to Hong Kong by the Englishlanguage The China Punch, the first cartoon magazine produced in Hong Kong. The magazine was created by British journalists and aimed at Englishspeaking readers, who would have been familiar with its style of satirical caricature, borrowed from the British Punch. The China Punch's content often maligned Chinese customs while praising the superiority of British culture, but it also combined Western and Chinese elements, and as such is early printed evidence of Hong Kong's East-meets-West culture. The cover of the first issue, for example, depicts a Westerner in the costume of a Qing government official, his arms open to welcome the reader. The symmetrical design of dragons holding a circular plate mimics the British seal while the dragons, landscape, and bamboo are Chinese.

## 5.

## The Situation in the Far East, 1899 Art: Tse Tsan-tai

The first cartoon created by a Chinese artist in Hong Kong, this cartoon depicts the political dangers then facing China. Various animals represent the countries that had a presence in China: a bear for Russia, a dog for Britain, a frog for France, an eagle for the United States, a round sausage for Germany, and a sun for Japan. A Qing government official waves invitingly at the Chinese border. Near Beijing, another official lounges calmly. Below him, a wealthy citizen enjoys a lavish meal. Together, these three figures illustrate the nonchalant attitude of the people and the government toward the situation.

Because the Qing government censored it in Hong Kong and China, Tse's drawing was printed in Japan. It was reprinted several times and widely circulated in China for many years, usually without credit to the artist. The best-known reprinting is in Alarm ofRussian Incident, a 1903 Shanghai-based anti-Russian publication. The artist later cofounded the South China Morning Post, the longest-running English-language newspaper in Hong Kong.

## 6-7.

## Man Fu Daily, 1909

## Art: Various artists

At the turn of the twentieth century, many Chinese newspapers included supplements that illustrated current events. These illustrations, usually line drawings made with a Chinese brush, are considered the beginning of Chinese cartoons. Although they were known by several different names, they can be grouped into three basic types: current events, satire, and humor.

# 事 廣

一葉落天下猴一座起大地有竟氣香本意氣不風派震也风流

壬子年十月中旬出版

![](images/00fcd1af33ab01cf720b31badddbb8105569a69a2fb299f4d729ef812e033ac9.jpg)

8–11.

## The Journal of Current Pictorial, 1905

## Art: Various artists

First published in Guangzhou, this magazine was part of the first generation of Chinese cartoon periodicals and thus played an influential role in the development of manhua. Published three times a month, it contained simple line drawings accompanied by text. (It also published poetry, short stories, and commentaries on current events.) Editorially, the publication was aimed at informing the general public of events and politics in China and promoting revolutionary anti-Qing ideas. The staff and many of its artists, including Pan Da-wei, Gao Jian-fu, He Jian-shi, Xie Ying-bo, and Chen Heng, belonged to the revolutionary group Chinese Alliance, which criticized Chinese feudal society, exposed the corruption of the Qing government, and decried China's weakness in the face of foreign powers. The Qing government banned the magazine in 1907; production resumed in Hong Kong for a few months the next year, but it was banned there, too, because Qing officials pressured the British colonial government. The artists then returned to Guangzhou, where they used pseudonyms to produce critical work such as Current Jokes Pictorial (Shi-xie Hua-bao) and People Pictorial (Ping-wen Hua-bao).

## 12–16.

## The True Record, 1912

Art: Various artists

This illustrated magazine was published in Guangzhou and distributed in Shanghai. Under the editorial guidance of Gao Qi-feng, The True Record used pictures and text to monitor the policies and politics of the government of the Republic of China, which had been established in 1911. It also reported current events and local news. The magazine's political drawings focused particularly on the power struggles and bureaucratic ineffectiveness of the Republic. In The Satirical Drawing, for example, the artist Zheng Nuquan implied that the new government, as led by provisional president Yuan Shi-kai, was incompetent. In another example, The Reasons for the Weakness of China, Ma Xing-tsi depicts China as a weak bureaucracy. In 1913, the government banned The True Record, and the artists and editor were forced to flee the country.

![](images/4b88bbed49f24ecf88f41dfe3d2aceb14a3e51e810a9af8cda4c5ec508be8765.jpg)

# 电漫 海

![](images/2430bfcb6191172879262729dda46a9f89c12bd2c0f7bff86d81f0cb8088b530.jpg)

![](images/fcb8dc4bbe4927bd6db2b4d2b499b9dcb41301f70ffb249d06756a8ac8f452f4.jpg)

![](images/51fa00a5db235227945af49fcbfdaf7ab8cd55837cbb46202909ff42737a76a8.jpg)

17–19.

## Renjian Pictorial, 1920 Art: Zheng Nu-quan

Renjian Pictorial is a collection of about 190 works by Zheng Nu-quan, one of the first-generation mainland-Chinese cartoonists. The artist was critical of traditional Chinese values and customs, and used cartoons to discuss topics such as “The Blemish of Early Marriages," “The Fault of Inadequate Family Education,” “The Vice of Opium Smoking," and “The Failing of Prostitution." The easily recognizable brush-and-ink style of Zheng's drawings is typical of Chinese cartoons of the time. Renjian Pictorial was published posthumously by the Leung Kwok-ying Drugstore in Hong Kong and given away free to customers. A block of text advertising various products appears at the top of each page.

## 20–26. Shanghai Sketch, 1928 Art: Various artists

Shanghai Sketch was one of the fist successful manhua magazines, selling about 3,000 copies a week. Each issue of the eight-page, tabloid-size newspaper included four pages of manhua, printed in two-color lithography. Published in Shanghai and edited by the famous manhua artists Ye Qian-yu and Zhang Guangyu, Shanghai Sketch included work by artists such as Huang Wen-nong and Zhang Zhen-yu. Some of its content was political. Power in Hand, by Huang Wennong, for example, shows Chinese president Jiang Jieshi with a fist out of proportion with his head and body, reflecting his control of the Nationalist Party. Shanghai Sketch also carried the first recurring fictional manhua character in China, Mr. Wang (Wang Xiansheng), created by Ye Qian-yu. Mr. Wang's focus on everyday life influenced the development of other manhua characters and story lines, but the plot of Mr. Wang also often reflected the political situation in China as well as the tension caused by an impending invasion by Japan.

![](images/63f394a9d1cef4814da64779061539dc1b3cfbd82e4d6984c176876452fde5e8.jpg)

![](images/49f94eed488ec202e4714bf6f26ba16d694c5c141c108fc8d45e0daf1e157ad1.jpg)

32-37.

Modern Sketch, 1934

Art: Various artists

This Shanghai-produced magazine was one of the most important manhua publications of the 1930s. Distributed throughout China, the magazine became a magnet for the best manhua artists, including Lu Shao-fei, Zhang Guang-yu, Huang Miao-zi, and Huang Wen-nong, as well as editor Lu Shao-fei. The cover of the first issue, drawn by Zhang Guang-yu, featured an ink-bottle knight riding a horse made of paper and pencils, implying that the magazine itself was courageous in challenging the evils of society. During its three years of publication, Modern Sketch’s artists shifted toward serious manhua about the impending Japanese invasion. Thirty-nine issues were published before Japan attacked China in July 1937.

33-40.

Shanghai Manhua, 1936 Art: Various artists

Although short-lived, Shanghai Manhua was one of the most influential cartoon magazines published in Shanghai. Its thirteen issues——the first published just a few months before the Sino-Japanese War broke out—included work by Zhang Ding, Liao Bing-xiong, and Huan Jun-wu, and introduced Sam-mo, by Zhang Le-ping, and Mr. Chan's Journey to Beijing, by Ye Quian-yu. As befitting the tenor of the times, much of the content was politically oriented, and addressed the inequalities emerging in Shanghai, China's first modern city.

![](images/a5fad9e14e336674a2cea1f77eb5fd41ca6c68d00b4920f3115dcf259420f2b9.jpg)

![](images/fcecbdc4220721407413cb36eeec930bfe8abf622564877555337dd14b7c876f.jpg)  
四月三十日假中華中學舉行预展招待文森界留影由有起前坐者：张正宇鄭家镇黄范子蛋光字黎永闯李凡夫徒立者：陳烟话陈酒信徐淫林璟戴望舒丁聰莱重風黄鼎柔淺于

封日本帝國主義的屈服面 Japanese imperialism submit 葉漫予Yeb Cbien-yu

新生的中國兒童林檎

41-46.

The Modern Chinese Cartoonist Exhibition catalog 1939

Art: Various artists

Before Hong Kong was occupied by Japan and the Pacific region was thrown into the Second World War, some of the Shanghai and Guangzhou artists who had fled to Hong Kong made the territory a center for anti-Japanese propaganda. Under the leadership of Ye Quian-yu, a branch of the National Cartoon Association was established in Hong Kong. In 1939, the group organized an exhibition of antiwar cartoons and comics, The Modern Chinese Cartoonist Exhibition. Participating artists included Zhang Zhen-yu, Huang Miao-zi, Cheng Ka-chun, Zhang Guang-yu, Lee Fanfu, Ting Chung, and Ye Quian-yu.

47-51.

This Is a Cartoon Era, 1948

Art: Various artists

During the civil war in mainland China, several Shanghai and Guangzhou cartoonists moved to Hong Kong. Once there, they made the territory a new center of manhua, organizing a cartoonists' club called Renjian Huahui and a cartoon studies department, and putting out This Is a Cartoon Era. Although they published just one issue, it revealed a moment of transition in the relationship between China and the rest of the world. For the cover, Zhang Guang-yu borrowed the cover images of Modern Sketch's first issue: an ink-bottle knight holding a pen fights the devil. Inside, Zhang and other artists, including Liao Bingxiong and Cheng Ka-chun, expressed their concerns about the relationship between China and the U.S., about the Nationalist Party, and about the unfairness implicit in capitalist society, among other topics.

## 52–61.

## New China Little Picture Books, 1950s–1960s Art: Various artists

After the Communists took power in 1949, the traditional mythological and costume genres of lianhuantu were no longer allowed on the mainland. However, the format itself was not banned, and several high-quality lianhuantu lauding Communist themes appeared. These manhua mainly expressed Communist views of power, the struggles of the poor under capitalism, and working-class heroism. Even the life story of Lenin was made into a lianhuantu.

## 62-64.

## Cry for the Death of a Crazy Man, 1955 Art/Script: Lui Yu-tin

Anti-communism was among the many topics Lui Yutin covered during the 1950s in his famous series Wulung Wong (King of Blunders). The example shown is not from the Wu-lung Wong series, but it does carry an anti-Communist message. The story depicts the power struggles in the Communist party, wherein people could accuse each other without reason. The story ends by saying all people involved in the Communist Party will go to hell after they die.

## 65-66.

## Three Men Going Home, 1955 Art/Script: Lui Yu-tin

Most of the anti-Communism comics series drawn by Lui Yu-tin in the 1950s were published by Asia Press Ltd., an American-funded publishing house. The story follows three American soldiers who make the mistake of moving to a Communist country because they admire its ideology. After arriving there, they discover the ugliness of the Communist system and with some effort manage to escape.

## 79.

![](images/6827be636b4d5c3fe8586eee91a6e030a0930f1a59f00b46a0a57f0a9a4dd1eb.jpg)

## 67-69.

The World of E-king Yen, 1968

Art/Script: Yen E-king

Yen E-King was a regular editorial cartoonist for the Dai Chung Daily and Express Daily, which usually dealt with international affairs. His work was well received by the public, and eventually collected into book form. The World of E-king Yen's thirty-four pages covered such topics and events as the Cultural Revolution, the Vietnam War, the depreciation of the British pound, the Civil Rights movement in the U.S., and conflicts in the Middle East.

## 70–71.

Four People Thematic Caricatures, 1970

Art/Script: Lam Jong-git, Nam Ying, Heung-shan Awong, Chau Hang-jok

This book collected work originally published in the Sing Tao Night Daily by editorial cartoonists Lam Jong-git, Nam Ying, Heung-shan A-wong, and Chau Hang-jok. Their themes were usually realistic portrayals of Hong Kong life, such as these restaurant scenes.

## 72–74.

The Hundred Manhua Poems, 1983

Art/Script: Nam Hui-man

Nam Hui-man based these drawings on his studies in mainland China. Each manhua and simple poem is intended to illustrate a problem of Chinese Communist ideology, often in a sarcastic or critical tone. Nam's book includes strong critiques of the destruction of the Chinese economy and the suppression of free speech and thinking.

## 75–78.

Theater of 1997,1985

Art/Script: Zunzi, Ma Long, Local Boy

The main contributors to Theater of 1997 included top newspaper editorial cartoonists such as Zunzi, Ma Long, and Local Boy. Their book addressed such topics as political asylum and the Chinese-British negotiations over the 1997 handover of Hong Kong, and included satirical cartoons about Communist politics.

## 79-80.

Rather Die Than Change, 1990

Art/Script: Yat Mok

This trendy, liberal-minded strip, which first appeared in a daily Hong Kong newspaper in 1988, explored ideas about the political relationship between Hong Kong and China. The strip ceased after the June 4 crackdown in 1989. When the series was collected in a book the next year, the artist titled it Rather Die Than Change in tribute to those who died in Tiananmen Square.

## 81-82.

Black Material Comic Collection, 1989

Art/Script: Zunzi

In this book, Zunzi explored the student democracy protests and Tiananmen Square crisis of June 1989. He divided the comic into several categories—historical background, politics, etc.—and accompanied each with explanatory text. Any discussion of Tiananmen Square was firmly suppressed on the mainland, so the topic was considered sensitive, or “black material," in Hong Kong.

## 83-85.

100 Caricatures of China, Hong Kong, and Taiwan   
Politicians, 1993   
Art/Script: Zunzi

These caricatures of Chinese, Taiwanese, and Hong Kong politicians include key mainland figures such as Deng Xiao-ping, Mao Ze-dong, Li Pang, local politicians Martin Lee and Chris Patten, and Taiwanese leaders Jiang Jie-shi and Lee Dung-wei. Each caricature is presented as an individual postcard, with the politician's year of birth and title on the reverse. The postcards are perforated, so they can be removed from the book and mailed.

## 86-88.

Zunzi’s Manhua: Hong Kong, China, UK, 1994 Art/Script: Zunzi

The editorial cartoons in this collection were selected by the author from the period between 1991 and 1994. He takes a strong prodemocracy position and often satirizes the hard-line attitude of Chinese government toward political reform and human rights.

## 89–91.

Grandpa Deng I, 1994

Grandpa Deng II, 1997

Art/Script: Zunzi

The main character of this strip, originally published in Next Magazine, is Deng Xiao-ping, portrayed as a chubby, retired “grandpa” still actively controlling China through younger colleagues such as Li Pang. In addition to Grandpa Deng, other key political figures appeared in the strip, which satirized the incompetence of the officials and ridiculed their actions. The strip ran for about six years, until Deng's death.

# 過渡期'91～'92漫畫集

## 92-94.

Fifty Steps, 1988

Art/Script: Ma Long

This cartoon book collected works by Ma for a bi-monthly news magazine. It included two sections which covered “Issues in Hong Kong,” and “Issues of the Political Party.'

## 95–97.

Ma Long's Political Cartoons, 1990

Art/Script: Ma Long

This collection of editorial cartoons captured the political changes and climactic events before and during the June 4 incident in 1989. It included five sections: “International Issues," “Immigrants and Refugees,” “Basic Law Show," “Dragon in the Cage,” and “The Glory of Blood."

## 98–99.

Fan Dou Magazine, 1991

Editor: Ma Long

As the name suggests, this magazine tried to be bold and naughty to grab the audience's attention, even when commenting on political and social issues. Its main contributors as well as its editor were leading newspaper editorial cartoon artists.

## 100–101.

Mild Comix, 1991

Editor: Apink

Mild Comix used pictures without text to deliver its message. The contents varied, although most were the creation of the editor, Apink, who also drew newspaper comics. Only three issues were published.

## 102.

Transition Period 1991–1992, 1992

Art/Script: Zunzi, Ma Long, and Yat Mok This comic book collected some of the daily newspaper work done by Zunzi, Ma Long, and Yat Mok. Their political cartoons captured the often strange incidents that occurred during the official transition period leading up to the 1997 handover of Hong Kong.

## 103-104.

Law for Life, 1993

Art: Tou Wing-tak

Tou Wing-tak used manhua to help young people understand the Basic Law of Hong Kong. In Law for Life, a little monk asks questions of an older monk; his answers illuminate such concepts as the Basic Law, how Hong Kong law works, and human rights issues. Unlike most political cartoons, which adopted a negative attitude toward the impending switch to Chinese rule, Law for Life favored the transition.

![](images/9469f9acb90cdf6ac882f98fe59e417fbfd801e6301da0bcb03803b6ab836127.jpg)

## 105-107.

Fan Dou Collection: Record Book of Transition Period, 1996

## Art/Script: Ma Long

This book is a collection of more than 100 editorial cartoons by Ma Long. It included a number of sections such as “Return of Bad Law,” “The Last Democracy Train,"“Welfare Rose Garden," and “International Issues." Annotations were also included to provide background information on the development of the events related to the cartoons.

## 108–111.

## A Biography of Mr. Legislator, 1997 Art/Script: Zunzi

Mr. Legislator was another well-received cartoon character created by Zunzi and was based on the common features of a legislator in Hong Kong. This cartoon panel has been published in Ming Pao since 1993. T'he book is a collection of the works published from June 1993 to March 1997. The editorial format of the collection is presented as a daily planner including the dates when the work was published.

## 112–115.

## Special Administrative Region Manhua Magazine, 1999 Éditor: Ma Long

This manhua magazine, published quarterly, is funded by the Arts Development Council with the objective of assisting nonmainstream cartoon development in Hong Kong. Ma Long is the editor as well as the major contributing artist. He created a cartoon character, Mr. CEO Tung, for the three-panel cartoon strip. The character design is based on Mr. Tung Cheehwa, the Chief Executive of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region. Other editorial cartoons by Ma can also be found in this 160-page magazine. Other occasional contributing artists include Wang King, Tou Sam, Local Boy, Tin Pak, and Ah Ching.

![](images/3f92e47714c99a8b1acf4a7cf6112cad1a94232e598f5b5ed7dcc316ac7cd041.jpg)

the art of humor: comical manhua

For decades, humorous manhua made up one of the major genres of Hong Kong comics. Before the Second World War, most comical manhua came in newspapers, magazines, and comic books printed in Shanghai and Guangzhou, on the mainland. Since humor tends to be local, comical manhua from Guangzhou was more popular in Hong Kong, because Guangzhou is closer to Hong Kong, and the cities sharè a regional dialect. One of the most influential Guangzhou comical manhua was The Sketch (Bun-gok Manhua), which first appeared in 1929. Its style of line drawings and text balloons, with the text in a colloquial dialect used by the lower classes, was typical of the manhua produced in southern China.

This southern manhua style, as drawn by Ye Yin-quan, Lee Fan-fu, and other artists, had great influence on the comics created in Hong Kong after the war. The work of Hong Kong artist Yuan Bou-wan, including the famous characters Kiddy Cheung and Miss Lau, followed the Guangzhou style, as did other works of the era, such as Tou Jowai’s Man in Suit and Sze-to Dip’s Sporty Aunt. During the 1950s, the most common and popular venue for manhua was the daily newspaper; after enough strips had run, they would be collected and reprinted in an individual comic book.

In the mid-1950s, Guangzhou comics began to show a distinct, American influence, which soon spread to Hong Kong. One of the first local manhua magazines produced in a Western style was Manhua World, which debuted in 1956. The magazine not only provided lighthearted entertainment, but it also nurtured promising local manhua artists. Manhua World made comical manhua popular just as the population of Hong Kong boomed. The popularity of these comics spread to other genres, too, which helped boost the fortunes of titles such as Uncle Choi

In the early 1960s, humorous manhua began appearing in poorly produced, tabloid-size newspapers known as “penny papers." The first and most successful of these was named Caricature Weekly, though it proved so popular that it soon became a daily. Caricature Weekly also inspired a number of imitators, including New Comics Weekly, Entertainment News, Epoch Comic Weekly, Manhua Daily, Companion Manhua, The Cartoon and Comics Garden, and Radar Pictorial.

The best-known comical manhua character is Old Master Q, who resembles Mr. Wang, the first cartoon character in Chinese history. Old Master Q is still in print and attracts readers of al ages. Another classic comical manhua character——and one geared toward women—is Miss Thirteen Dot, who appeared in Lee Wai-chun's 13-Dot Cartoons. The female Chinese equivalent of the American Richie Rich, this millionaire's daughter used her family's money to finance a diverse array of adventures. As the 1960s wore on, a split developed in comical manhua, with traditional Mr. Wang-type characters on one side and modern characters such as Miss Thirteen Dot on the other. Works in the more modern vein included Careless Young Master, by Ng Sheng-gwan, and Handsome Dak, Miss Silly, and Young Couple by Hui Guan-man.

After Hong Kong's first television station began broadcasting in 1967, the role of comics as an entertainment form began to decline. In order to survive, many comical manhua introduced themes and stories influenced by and directly copied from television programs. The early 1970s also saw the rise of kung fu manhua, which soon dominated the market. There were some alternatives to TV-related and kung fu manhua; the most popular of these were Wong Sze-ma's comics, especially Ngau-chai.

In the 1980s, several kung fu comics artists crossed over into comical manhua. Wong Yuk-long’s Yuk-long Manhua Biweekly, for example, tried to capture a share of the funny comics market. While kung fu manhua continued to dominate the market into the 1990s, many artists did publish small editions of comical manhua. This small market was represented by Manhua Hard and Soft Angel Theater, by Ho Kin-chung, One Woman, Three Markets, by Chan Ya, and others, produced by such editorial cartoonists as Ma Long and Apink.

In the mid-1990s, a new generation of artists began creating graphic novels that were funny, although they were different from traditional comical manhua. These alternative manhua included works by Lai Tat-wing and Au-yeung Craig. Lau Lee-lee’s selfdescribed feminist manhua took the comical genre one step further, providing a humorous critique of serious issues such as gender inequality and political power struggles in Hong Kong. These innovative works paved the way for the alternative manhua artists who were active in the final years of the century.

## 1-8.

## The Sketch (Bun-gok Manhua), 1929 Editor: Ye Yin-quan

A year after China’s first cartoon magazine, Shanghai Sketch, was published, this competitor appeared in Guangzhou. Six issues of The Sketch were published each month; twelve issues made up a single volume. Although published in Guangzhou, it was widely available in Hong Kong, where it had been printed during its first six months. The main attraction of the early volume of The Sketch were the stories of Brother Ho and Mister Tai-shi, by Ye Yin-quan. Beginning with volume four, the cover was printed in full color, and the magazine took on a more cynical tone. From this point on, there were two types of content inside: First, there were realistic stories of Guangzhou life, including scenes of markets, pawnshops, funerals, and even singing on the street. Second, there was the more risqué side of The Sketch; pictures of women were used to attract readers. The Sketch was targeted at readers in southern China, which meant its style was quite different from Shanghai manhua. Its main contributors were Ye Yin-quan, Lee Fan-fu, Li Maoji, and Huang Meng-tian.

## 9-12.

## Modern Miscellany, 1930

## Art: Various artists

The bulk of this magazine was devoted to news, society and tourist photographs, and other editorial features. However, manhua were very important to its popularity. Each issue's inside back cover carried a twelve-panel comic strip, Ye Qian-yu's Mr. Wang. (Mr. Wang was the first Chinese manhua character; he originally appeared in Shanghai Sketch.) Modern Miscellany also included regular contributions by Zhang Guang-yu, Zhang Zhen-yu, Huang Wennong, and Shao Xun-mei.

17.

蔣總裁決心全力改革 國民黨不應哀老腐收

## 13.

Chau Yat-ching, 1946 Art/Script: Yu Ming

Chau Yat-ching was one of the first manhua published in Hong Kong. The main character, Chau Yat-ching, came from a wealthy family, but his life consisted of a series of humorous blunders. Chau also represented the modern young man, wearing Western suits rather than traditional Chinese clothes.

## 14.

Fong Tong-geng Teases Chan Mung-gat, 1946 Art/Script: Ng Fa-pang

This early comic followed the two title characters' competitive and collaborative adventures. Their costumes and bald heads dated from the Qing dynasty.

## 15–19.

Wu-lung Wong (King of Blunders), 1946 Art/Script: Lui Yu-tin

Wu-lung Wong first appeared in 1946 in Ghuangzhou's Chum Daily newspaper, and it soon became one of the most popular serial comics in southern China. The story, about life in postwar Guangzhou, criticized the unfairness of society and the incompetence and ugliness of Nationalist Party officials. After it appeared in the newspaper, Wu-lung Wong comics were collected in book form; sometimes several issues would be released in a month. Circulation soon jumped to 50,000 to 60,000 an issue, and distribution widened to include Hong Kong, Macau, and other locations in the Guangzhou province. After the 1949 Communist takeover of mainland China, Wu Lung-wong continued to be published in the Hong Kong newspaper Chung Sing Daily. From the comic’s position on the front page of the paper, it is clear that the series was very popular. It was also later made into a film.

## 20–21.

Man in Suit, 1947

Art/Script: Tou Jo-wai

The main character of this comic always wore a Western-style suit, which symbolized the “modern man" in late-1940s Hong Kong. The comic was a sarcastic critique of the well-dressed man, who took advantage of his friends and played around.

## 22.

Sporty Aunt, 1947

Art/Script: Sze-to Dip

Created in 1938 as a newspaper strip, Sporty Aunt related the romantic affairs of the pinup girl Third Auntie, the beautiful Ah-ling, Director Sheung, and Uncle Chan. The strips were collected in this book after the war.

![](images/dc2e28a6276d631073800d5d57477bbf13abc00867aeb4dedabe2bd02800b6c3.jpg)

## 23–24.

Hot-tempered Leung, 1947 Art/Script: Yuan Bou-wan

This title was published before World War II as a newspaper column by Yan Wu-fa in Red Green Daily, then made into a movie by Yu Leung. In 1947, Yuan drew this two-volume comic book from the movie version. The story deals with the everyday lives of ordinary people; colloquial language is used throughout.

## 25–31.

Kiddy Cheung, 1947

Art/Script: Yuan Bou-wan

The character Kiddy Cheung was created by Yuan Bou-wan for a daily newspaper comic before the war and continued to be popular afterwards. Yuan used the child character to critique the adult world and reflect on the unfairness of society. In 1950, this manhua was made into a movie with Bruce Lee as Kiddy Cheung, his first film appearance. The movie was a hit and inspired more strips in a daily newspaper. These strips were reprinted in 1952 in the book Kiddy Cheung Extension

32–35.

Miss Lau, 1948

Art/Script: Yuan Bou-wan

Miss Lau was as popular as Yuan’s other comics, Kiddy Cheung and Arrogant Chiu. The story follows a pretty maid named Miss Lau and the men who pursue her. Yuan had close ties to the Hong Kong film industry, and Miss Lau became the basis of a 1949 movie that broke box office records when it premiered.

## 35.

Arrogant Chiu, 1948

Art/Script: Yuan Bou-wan

The character Sergeant Chiu first appeared in Red Green Daily, and the work was later published in booklet form. The comic book seen here, Arrogant Chiu Meets Four-eyes Choi, is full of humorous stories and lighthearted incidents.

## 36.

Wong Chai, 1949

Art/Script: Yuan Bou-wan

This Yuan Bou-wan comic follows Wong Chai, a spineless young man who cowers before his wealthy uncle. The story is funny—usually showing Wong Chai entertaining his stepmother and other female relatives——yet it also emphasized the gap between Hong Kong's upper and lower classes.

55.

37.

Manhua, 1953

Editor: Chan Chi-dor

This comic book collected work by several of Hong Kong's best manhua artists, including Chan Chi-dor, Chan Lou-nik, Au Ching, and Lui Yu-tin. It also included pirated reprints of Western comics.

38-47.

Manhua World, 1956

Editor: Mak Ching

Manhua World, a sixteen-page monthly magazine, was typical of popular mid-1950s publications. It contained a variety of features, including short stories, songs, and photos, as well as comics drawn by Cheng Ka-chun, Lee Fan-fu, Lee Ling-hon, Chan Chi-dor, and others. In general, the manhua in this magazine were influenced by American comics, yet some artists maintained a Chinese flavor. Each issue's cover explored a different format or kind of story. In addition to publishing well-known manhua artists, the magazine contained work by rising stars such as Wong Chak, Wong Sze-ma, Wu Shu-yu, Hui Guan-man, Heung-shan A-wong, Mak Ching, and Sung Samlong, all of whom later published individual comic books. Manhua World ran for almost eight years, with about seventeen issues published annually.

48–55.

Joke Comics, 1958

Editor: Hui Guan-man

Hui Guan-man was the biggest contributor to this comic, which featured his stories Grandpa and Granddaughter, Handsome Dak, Little Po Po, and Young Couple. Hui's target audience was older children and teenagers, who liked his fun and cheerful drawing style and stories. The magazine later merged with another title and in 1971 became 1,001 Joke Comics, with the added contributions of Ng Kam-hung and Mak Man-chung.

# 谢銷推機乘

60.

![](images/5771ee413fcd12effae9266ad9644f1ccba6e9b389ddb5c3bffe900abac2fda7.jpg)

![](images/72adfa21b1362aea92e7b606508176b20eb0185d76d3630c16791e738eb1b5af.jpg)

## 56.

## Caricature Weekly, 1961

## Editor: Lee Ling-hon

The same artists who had made the best-selling Manhua World created this tabloid-size weekly. Known as a “penny paper” (because it cost one cent), it became one of most successful comics of its time. Its eight pages contained mainly illustrations and comics, some in single panels and some in four-panel strips, as well as some fiction and a page of works by students. Caricature Weekly proved so popular that it began to publish every day, as Caricature Daily.

## 57.

## New Comics Weekly, 1962

## Editor: Tsui Ngai

This weekly penny paper was modeled after Caricature Weekly but was drawn by different artists, including Lee Yu, Lui Yu-tin, and Sung Sam-lung. Its contents were similar—cartoons, serial comics, and entertainment news——and like its predecessor, it quickly became a daily. The most popular strips in New Comics were Little Couple, by Lee Yu, Wu-lung Wong, by Lui Yu-tin, and Uncle Fatty, by Sung Samlung.

## 58–61.

## Entertainment News, 1963

## Editor: Fung Tai-wai

This two-color tabloid-size paper sold for ten cent and essentially started “penny paper fever” in Hong Kong. Among its eight pages was a gallery of Western (mainly American) comics, which were simply reprinted with the text in Chinese, as well as local comic strips and columns. Entertainment News also encouraged readers to submit their own comics——-and paid for them—which added to the paper's popularity. A supplement to the paper, Cartoon Daily, was published in 1963; its main appeal was adventure comics. Although artists were only paid about fifty cents per comic, the paper's insatiable demand for art—enough to fill four pages every day—kept many of them in business. The publisher supplied the story, and artists only had to draw the illustrations. Although the quality of the comics was poor, the constantly changing narratives kept readers buying the paper. The comics were later reprinted in a four-by-six-inch book that sold for fifty cents.

## 62.

## Epoch Comic Weekly, 1962

## Editor: Leung Tak-wah

This penny paper followed the typical pattern: eight pages oflighthearted and humorous stories, many with uplifting themes designed to help readers temporarily escape their problems. It became a daily paper in 1965.
