![](images/5237495dfd55d6875deacf773b88e775bb88de99a00d0ed66b8e697b51dc5e99.jpg)  
70.

## 63-64.

## Manhua Daily, 1963

## Editor: Mak Ching

This eight-page penny paper included work by local artists and one page of translated Western cartoons. One of its main attractions was that the front and back pages were printed in color. Martial arts and adventure comics were inside, and single-panel cartoons filled up the remaining space.

## 65.

## Radar Pictorial, 1965

## Art: Mok Kwan-ngok and Man Yong

A popular Japanese samurai movie, Blind Knight, inspired local artists Mok Kwan-ngok and Man Yong to create this eight-page daily paper, which featured a ongoing sword fighting comic. Like the movie, it was a hit, although the demands of drawing eight pages a day meant that the quality was very crude. Still, it remained popular until the mid-1970s, making it one of the longest-running penny papers. Mok and Man started another penny paper, Ultraman Pictorial, an eight-page daily based on the Japanese manga of the same name. However, this paper was short-lived since the artist could not draw enough for two dailies at the same time.

## 66-67.

## Companion Manhua, 1963

## Editor: Unknown

This penny paper ran imitations of work by famous contemporary comic artists, including Hui Guanman, Chan Chi-dor, Mak Ching, Lee Ling-hon, Wong Chak, and Wong Sze-ma. However, it also helped create a new genre of manhua when it combined cartoon strips with adventure stories.

## 68-70.

## The Cartoon and Comics Garden, 1964

This penny paper was another eight-page tabloid with color covers. It included the work of Lee Singcheung, Ng Gei-ping, and the rising female artist Lee Wai-chun.

![](images/1e39b713a1d55ef1fd6e280d5823be003c494de4de19c07d609da277a58ee9c2.jpg)

71-103.

Old Master Q, 1964

Art/Script: Wong Chak

Old Master Q is the most popular manhua character in Hong Kong as well as in Chinese communities all over the world. Almost four decades after the comic's debut, Old Master Q is still being published, each issue eagerly read by a worldwide audience. Old Master Q was even the subject of popular live-action and animated films in the 1960s and 1970s. The comic itself is a four-panel strip in which Old Master Q and his sidekicks (“Big Dumb” or “Big Potato” and Mr. Chun) get involved in many different kinds of adventures. Whether he's traveling through time, visiting Japan, or marrying one of many wives, Old Master Q frequently finds himself in trouble. The character of Old Master Q was greatly influenced by Ye Qian-yu’s Mr. Wang, who first appeared in 1928 in Shanghai Sketch. It was also possibly influenced by Peng Di’s Lao Fu Zi, who was popular in the 1930s and 1940s in mainland China.

104–108.

Old Master Q's Crazy Comics, 1965 Editor: Wong Chak

Following the success of his character Old Master Q, Wong Chak and a few other young artists published this variety magazine, which was inspired by American comics. Each issue featured eight pages of sixpanel Old Master Q comics. The remaining four to six pages had short comics by Ching Ho, Ng Hocheung, and Sze-to Yong, among others.

## 109.

Crazy World, 1966

Editor: Wong Chak

The main lure of this thirty-six-page magazine was Wong Chak's Old Master Q, although it also included comics by Heung-shan A-wong, Tsui Shing-on, Tou Sam, and Ma Chung. It was never as popular as Old Master Q’'s Crazy Comics, and its short run ended with the 1967 riots, which disrupted Hong Kong society and caused huge setbacks for the manhua industry.

## 110–112.

Fat Chan, Gentleman, and Brother Ho Manhua, 1965 Art/Script: Lee Fan-fu

This comic was created by Lee Fan-fu, a manhua artist of the older generation who was trying to be part of the 1960s comics boom. A collection of Lee's prewar work, this publication was targeted toward adults and thus had difficulty competing with all-ages comics such as Old Master Q. Only about ten issues were published.

## 113–114.

Dumb Detective Manhua Series, 1965

Art/Script: Sung Sam-lung

Each issue of Dumb Detective revolved around stories about the title character and his assistant, Fat Chu, as they try to solve crimes, with often humorous results. The series ran for more than 200 issues.

## 115–116.

First published in Sun Daily as a four-panel strip, this serial followed the adventures of Dummy Seven, a not-so-bright character who lives among smart people. The other character, A-ling, or “Beauty” (although her name means “beauty," she is not beautiful), has a quick, sarcastic tongue and likes to take advantage of people, especially Dummy Seven. Only three issues were printed.

117–132.

![](images/5a189740316b42bef35dbf60ac728bb01aee4fb30e083d54e9d97033dec73271.jpg)  
131.  
132.

13-Dot Cartoon, 1966

13-Døt was the first major comic created especially for female readers. Drawn by a woman and featuring female characters, the comic helped project a modern, Western image to young women in Hong Kong. The title character, Miss Thirteen Dot, who is based loosely on the American Richie Rich, is a millionaire's teenage daughter. She is pretty, popular, and compassionate, and uses her money to bankroll wild adventures, to learn skills such as bullfighting and ballet dancing, and for charity. Some of Miss Thirteen Dot's appeal was her fashion sense; young women sometimes copied her clothing style.

Art/Script: Lee Wai-Chun

![](images/05d9342f0ece365008ef7871a11259c5f3569a5280ef35a4a5cc20d6c20dd5d3.jpg)

![](images/6192ba60088af3f3b7a876181bf0b6c9f2806d33e7bbe7e89a864671af9d1277.jpg)

![](images/adec80c1c29cef29af33ec5f47115fa810b839d6f973ad150c02f6706237b443.jpg)

## 133-134.

Careless Young Man, 1966 Art/Script: Ng Sheng-gwan

This comic followed the life of a wealthy young man. He is handsome, wears modern clothes, and has a perfect rich father, a caring mother, a maid, and a girlfriend. However, his absentmindedness leads him into many comical adventures and provides fodder for many jokes.

## 135.

Dai-wah and Siu-wah, 1966 Art/Script: Lee Shek-cheung

This manhua strip was originally published in a pro-China newspaper. Its stories—about two brothers, one mature and considerate and the other cute and energetic—had positive messages but also carried Communist ideas and anti-American propaganda.

## 136.

Movie Stars and Comics Magazine, 1967 Editor: Youth Culture Committee As its title suggests, this manhua magazine integrated entertainment news with comics. It is one of the first comics tied to TV and movie stars.

## 137.

Handsome Dak, 1967   
Art/Script: Hui Guan-man   
First published in Joke Comics and Sun Wan Daily, this comic included light, humorous stories about its handsome main character and his various girlfriends.

## 138.

MCC Cartoons, 1968

Editor: Fung Siu-ho

The title of this comic, which lasted for only one issue, was slang for mung cha cha (“uncertain of anything”). Its artists were trying to capitalize on Hong Kong popular culture, with an emphasis on new fashions and dancing.

## 139.

New Mr. Wang and Mr. Chu, 1968

Art/Script: Ching Fu and Mak Man-chung The main character of this comic, Mr. Wang, was named after the character created in 1928 by Ye Qianyu for Shanghai Sketch. Its two artists hoped that recreating classic manhua characters would let them compete with Old Master Q.

## 140-141.

Mr. Chu and Aunt Eight, 1970 Art/Script: Mak Man-chung

T'he format and style of Mr. Chu and Aunt Eight was similar to Old Master Q with six-panel strips. Although the events, jokes, and tricks involved were all related to Hong Kong, they were rather ordinary.

# 即播電視

![](images/211afae83f1b375873fc917d7e9b37f12ecb3f19fdcf4acb0c543a6d970727d9.jpg)

150.

![](images/706a6acd6d0d3b732ca9709898682004f5b397b2550611c306f9c7560ad1462b.jpg)

## 142.

Happy Family TV Episode Comics, 1969 The introduction of television to Hong Kong caused many manhua fans to lose interest in comics. However, it also created a new genre of comics in redrawn versions of TV shows.

## 143.

Happy Fruit, 1970 Editor: Ng Sheng-gwan

Happy Fruit published short stories and six-panel strips devoted to entertainment and humor. The main attraction was Mr. Careless, a character the editor and major contributor, Ng Sheng-gwan, had created previously. In addition to Ng's work, Lee Wai-chun's 13-Døt appeared in every issue; Miss thirteen Dot is a close friend of Mr. Careless.

## 144-145.

Sweet and Gentle, 1970 Art/Script: Tse Ling-ling

This title was similar to Lee Wai-chun's 13-Dot Cartoon in its emphasis on the main character's appearance. Also like Lee’s work, Sweet and Gentle focused on fashion and featured beautiful clothes. Although the main character is Chinese, she has blond hair.

146–147.

Grandpa and Granddaughter, 1971 Art/Script: Hui Guan-man

The character Grandpa Chow was very similar to Old Master Q, right down to his traditional Chinese clothing and his sense of humor. The stories revolve around his cute granddaughter and were considered appropriate for all ages.

## 148.

Miss Silly, 1971 Art/Script: Hui Guan-man

After 13-Dot proved such a success, Hui tried to capture a share of the female market. 'The overall style of Miss Silly was more influenced by American comics, but the humor was not as funny, nor was the fashion as glamorous.

## 149.

Young Couple, 1971 Art/Script: Hui Guan-man

The story follows Handsome Dak, his wife, and their new baby. It was aimed at the large audience of baby boomers, who were beginning to have children of their own.

150–151.   
Instant TV, 1973   
Editor: Lai Man-fung   
This short-lived magazine imitated the format of TVB Weekly, a news magazine put out by Television Broadcast, one of Hong Kong's major TV stations.

154.

契爺 與 大小姐

![](images/c434d12e359e469cb65bfb0b7dcee115d4f3fdf1b00bd370b43b9392f088cb7c.jpg)

![](images/3495bfc5d884b33e2a2b426e1492dfd79bdf74f2b477988cfa53f85294f0ccee.jpg)  
153.

![](images/26a5bc7938a86f2ed97106e0b543c8805b3c78b0c3ce8895f15192d4f19a8cc9.jpg)

契能牛仔 大小姐

![](images/7ff5da178c6f17c46dcc1b396784f53f9b097170fa44c71b3261ac326cc0d9f5.jpg)  
155.

![](images/2ab9fd31938891ea75638f8fbafd71edc7f848db7e733ed1864b5724c62cc99b.jpg)  
156.

![](images/1d5f2952779842bf2c798d87e77a0437fbf41d7edcfe36f82823b965678cb4c8.jpg)  
157.

![](images/7f33dd5d77d3c3ed1d5695c2234c6133d7eaf75fcbfef7ce8f32b0cb40c106e4.jpg)  
158.

159.

163.

![](images/84da6a3301c10fb97ed1b846134a79bfc43b00694dc42d2204cd2f21c0a0e55d.jpg)

![](images/7260354a1f31a7a46b5d11793ef7da59be78de50d1ca815a00b83965ce03d30b.jpg)

![](images/405516857853601cbd101fffa94818c444bfa826470e63525fb18ddede5bcc3a.jpg)

![](images/9bcf5e4612b033a92c0a2e7fdbd92f629077a9a9da8f3e2e3a1473cc5e814793.jpg)

![](images/20e2ecb57c35472990aa57944db56bf0cf3493d3e6c271e25ed5ca4937ad1501.jpg)

![](images/7e9ffd060642a9f5665f2ab155d8d896127a4241f22a2f4d707fa456d9402cd4.jpg)  
165.

![](images/e3eb4d21bfc518aa1cbf34556f40db8bbe191a6ad5033605fb4bba05b3a476fa.jpg)

![](images/00a5be4a68502b84a0ca1e2ec72838187d08169be96f07b4b079d4131898958e.jpg)  
166.

![](images/1b0f1bb36628350ed69b7e54ba548e8153cf44a1ca8c942997038c36c90fb1bd.jpg)

## 152.

Father and Ms., 1968

Art/Script: Wong Sze-ma

This cartoon was first published in the newspaper Ming Pao as a four-panel strip. It originally focused on Father and one of his colleagues, Ms.; Father's adorable son, Ngau-chai, also appeared from time to time. In response to its readers’ preferences, the comic's focus was shifted toward warmhearted stories of father and son.

## 153.

Father and Son, 1971

Art/Script: Wong Sze-ma

This four-panel strip had no text, just action sequences. Beginning with this series, Wong's comics focused on the father-son relationship and included stories that revealed lessons about life. The covers also emphasized the caring relationship between father and son.

154–156.

Father, Ngau-chai, and Ms., 1972

Art/Script: Wong Sze-ma

The story of this four-panel strip takes place in the office and at home. When Ngau-chai became more popular, Ms. was eventually phased out.

157–158.

My Boy, 1980

Art/Script: Wong Sze-ma

My Boy was a collection of approximately one hundred strips that represented Wong Sze-ma's best work from the 1970s. Themes included love and the close relationship between a father and son.

159–160.

Ngau-chai, 1982

Art/Script: Wong Sze-ma

Ngau-chai was a collection of the comics Wong Szema drew at the peak of his productivity and popularity. The four-panel strips mostly followed the relationship between a father and his adorable, understanding, and energetic son. The series was published until 1983, when Wong Sze-ma died of cancer.

161–167.

Ngau-chai's Collection, 1983

Art/Script: Wong Sze-ma

After Wong Sze-ma's death, one of Hong Kong's biggest publishers put out this forty-one-volume, 132- strip collection of his work. Each issue was numbered and had a subtitle; issue two, for example, was called “I Want a Star,” issue four was “Father Can Do Anything,” and the final issue was “Good-bye Ngau-chai." The series published most of Wong's Ngau-chai comics, but it did not publish them in chronological order, nor did it indicate when the strips were originally created.

![](images/c7a076e9445a337eb4250494f8de726ef99a395da53bd4df7fc3e58afd649bf2.jpg)

![](images/9b6736b873c6d10d007c08a27594ad2206c92cb5403f9f75ec297b6547032c51.jpg)  
爆石狂想  
希感對面地壁爆的是全编。

168–171.

Colored Ngau-chai Selections, 1987

Art/Script: Wong Sze-ma

This series of Ngau-chai reprints was a tribute to Wong Sze-ma by several famous writers, including Yik Shu, A-nung, Lee Ying-hou, and Choi Lan. Each author gave a description of a strip, since the originals were free of any text. The comics themselves were colored for the first time. Although the explanation of each individual strip may have been helpful, the descriptions were later criticized for restricting the reader's imagination and damaging the artist's original meaning. Only five issues were published.

172–174.

Colored Ngau-chai Collection, 1989

Art/Script: Wong Sze-ma

This series reprinted the Colored Ngau-chai Selections without the descriptive text. The added color, however, was not always well executed or planned. The publisher also marketed a wide variety of Ngau-chai products, including stickers, coloring books, calendars, and pencil sharpeners, and redesigned the format to include quizzes, greeting cards, and other features. These additions were quite popular with its young audience.

175–177.

Gorgeous Susan, 1983

Art/Script: Wong Sze-ma

Gorgeous Susan was another major work by Wong Szema; this collection was published after his death. The four-panel strip follows the life of Susan, an attractive secretary, through the trials of office politics and her relationships with male colleagues.

178–180.

Debussy, 1983

Art/Script: Wong Sze-ma

These comics were drawn by Wong Sze-ma under the pseudonym Debussy for the newspaper Ming Pao. They had a critical and satirical approach to politics and social situations, and an emphasis on contemporary life in Hong Kong. Wong illustrated the city's crowded living conditions, the incessant construction and noise, and the effects of current events, such as the 1983 depreciation of the Hong Kong dollar.

上官小強主羅

![](images/6309c911a8e08fa41952d3337ec28f01d11ddeb1ed023540b26ffdbe6f85be0f.jpg)

# 星期日 漫書插圖設計作品期期有

![](images/b9ab8a1c18c33e03fa51bb229cbf21a134a58c159524a4eb87022d5f7f74cd2e.jpg)

188.

星期日 漫庫設計作品

![](images/6f3a68b8f2549c9ff432d4b781e33c5d8144fb73beebf15e509e60bc586c922e.jpg)  
189.

## 181.

Ms. Carefree, 1974

Art/Script: Ng Gam-hong

This comic reflected changes in how women were seen in Hong Kong; it features a modern, sexy female character influenced by television programs such as Charlie's Angels. The strip first appeared in a newspaper and was later reprinted as this booklet.

## 182.

New Wave Manhua Magazine, 1980

Editor: Tony Chan

In response to the “new wave” of Hong Kong film, a group of young artists, including Ma Wing-shing, Chu Jo-yee, and Sham Lak-hung, published this magazine with the hopes of creating a new kind of manhua. The most popular comics of the time were violent, fighting manhua, so this magazine gave Hong Kong comics a new and fresh perspective.

## 183-185.

Siu-keung Manhua Collection, 1981

This collection, put together by Seung-gun Siu-keung and a group of young artists, includes Sau-sing Chai (Longevity Boy), by Seung-gun, and the comic-book version of Jackie Chan’s film Drunken Master, by Ma Wing-shing. It also published the work of many rising artists. Since most comic books were collections of strips that had appeared in penny papers, the cover stressed that the contents had never before been published. Covers also used imagery from popular films such as Alien to attract readers.

## 186.

Humor World, 1981

Editor: Yeung Wai-pong

The majority of this publication's contributors were newspaper or editorial cartoon artists, including Tao Sam, Yat Muk, Zunzi, Yan Ting-hau, Mok Wah, Au Ching, and Lee Tak-hong. Its content was mostly cartoon strips, almost all drawn in à single-line style.

## 187.

Manhua New Trend, 1981

Editor: Cheung Wai-kit

One of many short-lived humor magazines, this title combined four-panel strips and short stories. The cover of the first issue used caricatures of movie stars to try to appeal to new readers.

## 188–189.

Sunday Comics, 1982

Editor: Ho Tai-yi

This variety weekly collected cartoons, illustrations, and comics. Although it was created by local artists, it's contents revealed how much Hong Kong manhua were becoming influenced by Japanese manga. Major contributors included Chu Jo-yee, Tang Punbong, Ko Cheung, and Fung Chi-ming.

# 王郎漫畫

![](images/aacb10223173ca105bf69527f210770bd9c9d3a4af9d9e4401b282f98b6e5200.jpg)

# 鞋底秋画傳

![](images/79232c7c8c1ed8183e6495045914a4b9485f778e88a9e10d19a3227826f39cc6.jpg)

![](images/14dd19899d7b3070a9d985a60b84c43179b7457c2374719801d3b55a3ad791bc.jpg)

![](images/f7070b37784143983e4a171b5ca696fc7b9dde6c84d8fa09a6d3e876413a7e18.jpg)

## 190–192.

2001 Comics Weekly, 1982

Editor: 2001 Editorial Board

This magazine featured a mix of manga and local comics. The Japanese comics it carried, such as Doctor Slump and Crying Killer, were the most popular manga at the time. Contributors included Chu Jo-yee and other relatively unknown locał artists.

## 193-196.

Yuk-long Manhua Biweekly, 1984

Producer: Kie Man-kin

This magazine had two stars: Wong Yuk-long, the comics publisher and artist, and Yuan Dai-yong, the era's highest-paid caricaturist. Each cover featured a drawing of Wong Yuk-long with a different celebrity, which attested to Wong's own celebrity. The biweekly magazine primarily ran lighthearted stories and humorous comics, but since Wong was trying to build his relationship with the entertainment industry, it also included regular coverage of entertainment news as well. It also introduced the drawing style of kung fu and adventure manhua artists to a more mainstream audience.

197–198.

Hai-dai Chau Pictorial, 1983

Art/Script: Dong Pui-sun

Hong Kong comedian Charles Ng inspired the look and attitude of this comic's main character, while the stories took a humorous approach to social issues and personal relationships. The comics in this short-lived, self-published collection first appeared in the Sun Daily's manhua column.

## 199.

One Is Nice, 1983

Art/Script: Tou Sam

This lighthearted and humorous series was first published in the newspaper Oriental Daily. The action revolves around a little boy similar to A-giu, who appears in the 1990 comic Three Little Guys.

201.

205.

![](images/03172b1c6088b6eccf6c4ae278cb25612c61f7f7940596495c83411ba1bb5342.jpg)

# 界世画漫漫画少年 3.00

![](images/f253729e64794dafa1a7f9e2c3f1c40dbf23e619fe8710086262afc2658549bb.jpg)

![](images/2bc4170ad92c9f3d64be08a7f072991745a48717759b10637fddecdb3ace8eb9.jpg)

![](images/928c628365353b176e8d22ea2ba106bea7637017d08c22e9185e30a05f5f3994.jpg)

![](images/37fa79950020fed07545eacfe3bb4355471ac2b9c3e26a678ae6cc60e343ab82.jpg)  
210.

![](images/42843be80ab9ec606fee88c1ba1d98f8c5f2cab32e32235185db13396e68be7d.jpg)

Manhua by Lee Dak-hong, 1984   
Art/Script: Lee Dak-hong   
This collection of more than 156 cartoons by Lee Dakhong was the artist's first manhua publication. His next collection was called Little Funny Booklet and was very similar to this one. 203-204.   
Onion Head Manhua Series, 1984   
Art/Script: Lam Chun-keung   
This series was first published in Ming Pao Weekly and was a kind of combination of comics and poetry. The main character had an onion-shaped head and liked to talk to himself. 205.   
The Four Treasures of Dragon Gate, 1986   
Art: Cai Zhi-zhong   
The title of T’he Four Treasures of Dragon Gate, a collection of the artist's early works, refers to four different characters. 206.   
Manhua Zhuang-zi: Natural Sound of a Common Artemisia, 1986   
Art: Cai Zhi-zhong

In this illustrated version of the ancient Chinese work of philosophy Zhuang-zi, appealingły drawn characters accompany the concise original text. Because it was so easy to read and provided a new approach to classic Chinese philosophy, this comic received a great deal of attention and eventually broke sales records in both Hong Kong and Taiwan. A series of manhua based on ancient Chinese philosophy and literary works followed, along with English versions published in Singapore.

Comic World Bimonthly, 1987   
Editor: Seung-gun Siu-wai   
This imitation of Yuk-long Manhua lacked the original’s sophistication. Its main strip was The Legend of Siu-wai, drawn by the editor. 208.   
Comic Pig, 1988   
Editor: Wong Kwok-hing   
Wong Kwok-hing, who already had a reputation for humorous cartoons, provided most of the comic strips and columns for this publication. In addition to comics, Comic Pig included entertainment news. 209–210.   
Comics for City Boy, 1988   
Editor: Hui King-sam   
This serial was another magazine started by younger manhua artists. It primarily covered Japanese popular culture and was aimed at young readers.

215.

# 小豆丁

![](images/1bd8169503ea3d1102669af2a2a3da5af58a4e74bf12760119928965731adfd0.jpg)

![](images/ecdb414d62e9840e43b64af89e66e76dcd54b78eed977419c060fafb4bc96507.jpg)  
219.

211.

Chu-yi Sing Comics Collection, 1988 Editor: Dong Pui-sun

A famous businessman, Yau Da-shing, inspired the creation of Chu-yi Sing, the main charecter of this comic. Although it originally related the good side of businessmen, the focus later shifted to more ordinary, working-class people. Politically, the comic supported common citizens and included mild critiques of social inequality.

212.

Manhua Hard and Soft Angel Theater, 1989 Art: Ho Kin-chung

Script: Hard and Soft Angel

Hard and Soft Angel were popular radio disc jockeys who had gained attention and an audience with their loud banter and sarcastic jokes. They both had backgrounds in graphic design and friends who were already drawing comics, so their transition to the manhua world was easy.

213-214.

Media Tour of Cahiers Du Cinéma, 1989 Editor: Unknown

This comic book featured work by young, alternative manhua artists—including Ho Ka-chiu, Jek na-si, and TSSW Leung—who wanted to communicate their thoughts and ideas on contemporary life. Graphically, their comics experimented with different styles and formats. Although they started the comic with high hopes, the project was short-lived.

215–217.

One Woman, Three Markets, 1989 Art/Script: Chan Ya

Chan Ya was a newspaper columnist, and this was her first and only attempt to use comics to express her opinions on issues of gender politics. Her strip first appeared in a pro-China newspaper in early 1989. The political climate was relatively liberal at the time, and she was able to criticize the mainland government, especially about the democracy protests in Beijing. The climate shifted drastically after the June 4, 1989, massacre in Tiananmen Square, and the strip ended. The main character was an unattractive woman who made straightforward observations about the world around her. One Woman, Three Markets was a rare attempt by a female artist to produce a comic strip on serious political issues.

218–220.

Three Little Guys, 1990 Art/Script: Tou Sam

Three Little Guys follows the adventures of three naughty boys, led by A-giu, a similar version of whom appears in the artists’ earlier comic One Is Nice. Here, the artist tried to depict the adult world from a kid's perspective.

# 好小子米米羅永康

利志達 關烈文 BEN 温永聰 鄧耀輝 ·主筆 曾憲璋 意彩糖

![](images/9fe7209bb9e742568b1c41c115eb2d77bd127b85a4f2485b3548259786efa699.jpg)

![](images/0e6ca468ba670c1f66841f83fd2eaca0c3f067a9771416ef290903f53c9a2c3d.jpg)  
231.

## 221-224.

## Monthly Comic Magazine, 1990 Editor: Yuan Kin-toa

Although it claimed to be Hong Kong's first pure manhua magazine, it was actually the first serious critical magazine about manhua. It included a mix of reviews and commentary about Hong Kong manhua and reports on foreign comics. There were also features introducing new manhua artists such as Li Chitat, whose elaborately detailed drawings were praised by readers. Unfortunately, this rare example of a sincere magazine about manhua was short-lived, publishing only five issues in 1990 and three more in 1993.

## 225.

Tin Ha Magazine, 1991

Editor: Cola Boy

This magazine was also about comics, although it had a less serious tone than Monthly Comic Magazine. Named after Ma Wing-Shing's comics company Tin Ha Publishers, it reported manhua and manga industry news and discussed comics trends. It lasted for three years before folding in mid-1993.

## 226-227.

Humorous Cartoons by Ma Long, 1991 Art/Script: Ma Long

Ma was an active cartoonist for various daily newspapers. This book collects the single-panel cartoons he drew in 1991; about 132 cartoons are included. The collection does not include his satirical works.

## 228–229.

Daily Pictorial, 1991

Editor: Unknown

This newspaper began just as the success of comics dailies was fading. Its aim was to print local comics, entertainment news, and the latest Japanese manga. Although it began with a big promotional splash, it only lasted for three days.

## 230.

Double Star Comics, 1993

Editor: Law Wing-hon

The young artists who produced this comic book were more interested in promoting themselves than making money. It was influenced by Japanese manga, but it had a more lighthearted, humorous tone.

## 231.

Comic Alley, 1993

Editor: Seung-gun Siu-keung

This magazine was led by Seung-gun Siu-keung, an experienced artist from the 1980s. He helped new artists experiment with and improve their drawing skills, and allowed the magazine to publish alternative and avant-garde comics.

## 232.

The Legend of Love, 1995   
Art/Script: Ma Long   
This comic book collected works originally published in newspapers. Unlike Ma Long's other editorial cartoon work, The Legend of Love deals with lighter subject matter in stories of love and dating.

## 233.

The Record of the Entertainment Industry, 1995 Art/Script: Apink This four-panel strip, originally published on the entertainment page of a daily newspaper, depicts stories of the dark side of the entertainment business.

234.   
Here Comes Boss Lee, 1995   
Art/Script: Apink   
This sarcastic and satirical comic, aimed at a workingclass audience, tellsstories of office politics.

## 235–245.

McMug Manhua Series, 1993 Art: Mak Ga-bik Script: Tse Lap-man

McMug was the most popular comics character in Hong Kong in the 1990s, with a devoted following among children and adolescents. The little pink pig, drawn with a thin, black line and watercolors, gets caught up in lighthearted and funny situations, and he is known for asking silly, simple questions. Although at first the comic was simple, Mak and Tse over time developed more elaborate story lines and sophisticated drawing styles.

## 246–250.

Art/Script: Lai Tat-wing

With this work, Lai Tat-wing broke away from the manga that dominated comics in the 1990s to create something original. Even the title is fresh: the words form a sound without any real meaning. His strips left much to the reader's imagination. For example, the 160-page comic Dan-dan Ha, Go-go Nut contains no dialogue at all. Lai’s highly controlled line drawings and composition, and his originality and enthusiasm were well received by readers. Today it is considered one of the most original Hong Kong comics of the 1990s.

## 251–254.

## Three Times Seven Equals Twenty-One, 1995 Art/Script: Au-yeung Craig

This alternative comic was a mixture of illustrations and comics drawn in untraditional formats. Each page of the ten chapters contains just one frame; the frames are broken into action sequences to tell the story. The book was printed in both Chinese and English, reflecting the bilingual culture of Hong Kong. Auyeung later published more highly original work in the series My Own Private Heaven, which maintained the bilingual format while experimenting more with traditional comic forms.

## 255–256.

## Mom's Drawer Is at the Bottom, 1998 Art/Script: Lau Lee-lee

This self-described feminist manhua was the first of its kind in Hong Kong. The artist challenged social and cultural norms by engaging feminist humor, and outlining the relationships amoong gender, humor, and feminist social critique. The 228-page collection included such topics as sex and sexuality, sex roles, gender politics, life, media, home, the nation, and the earth. Lau, however, did not draw her manhua in an explicit way, nor did she directly attack patriarchal ideology.

# 天花板上的貓與人

作者草日

257–264.

Sam's Manhua Series, 1995–2000

Art/Script: Choa Yat

The artist first began this manhua series in Ming Pao in 1993. Its light-hearted approach to the everyday life of ayoung couple was well received by young readers. The main character, Sam, is a typical family man, and his wife, Yan Yan is a typical wife who loves shopping. Their fat and cute housecat, Hamburger, is the main character of the series. The plot is about the fun things that happen in Sam's household and to Hamburger. Other characters——Fatty, Cream, Ah Lay— are cats, and Angela and Ronald are Sam's parentsin-law. The series has been published in six collections, including The Cat and the People on the Ceiling (1995), None of Your Cat Business (1996), Three Households and One Cat (1998), Supreme Cat (1999), Cat of the House (1999), and Cat Comes, Cat Goes (2000). Apart from the books, other merchandise, such as figurines of Sam’s family, a bag, a piggy bank, and a mug are also for sale.

265–271.

Leung's Saga Series, 1998–2001

Art/Script: Choa Yat

As the title suggests, the main characters of the cartoon strip are Leung's family: Mrs. Leung, Mr. Leung, and their three children, son, Song Song, and his wife, Yo Yo, first daughter, Mimi, and her husband, Kim Kim, and second daughter, Dor Dor, who is still in high school. The series is targeted at female readers with a plot that revolves around family values and love. It also reflects the everyday life of Hong Kong people. A collection of the series was first published in 1998 under the title The Leung Saga 1, and was followed by The Leung Sagas 2, 3, and 4.

![](images/7c358e52841a000be670f846b6f252898577f9ea62d4df056541eea0d4d938b7.jpg)

![](images/95438db618cfcb6d8530e80b208dc29e3b0f0290b885a7ff02fd437dfdb6ad68.jpg)

spirits of kung fu: action manhua

To most non-Chinese speakers, kung fu is simply a martial art made popular by Bruce Lee. Yet kung fu refers not only to martial arts, or wu-shu in Chinese, but also to the common spirit shared by various styles of wu-shu. The kung fu genre of Hong Kong manhua thus includes not only stories of hand-to-hand martial arts, but also sword-fighting epics, traditional Chinese myths, gangster stories, science fiction, and tales of historic fighting in full period costume.

Until the 1950s, Hong Kong depended on Shanghai for its supply of lianhuantu, the traditional illustrated storybook. The format of lianhuantu, sequential line drawings with descriptive text at the top of the page, remained almost unchanged until the end of the Second World War. By the 1920s and 1930s, Hong Kong readers were already familiar with the martial arts lianhuantu published in Shanghai.

After the Communist revolution, when the supply of lianhuantu from Shanghai dried up, the British territory began to develop its own comics. Hui Guan-man's 1958 lianhuantu, Uncle Choi, not only broke new ground in format and style but also marked a new direction in Hong Kong comics toward a more accurate depiction of real life. Other works, such as the adventure story Boy Scout (1960), by Ng Gei-ping, followed Uncle Choi's lead.

Still, during most of the 1960s, the Hong Kong comics market was dominated by penny papers and humorous comic books. The action and adventure titles that were available were mainly copies of American superhero comics and Japanese science-fiction manga. That began to change in 1968, when the work of Wong Yuk-long appeared. Wong's Little Vagabond, published in that year, was immediately popular. Two years later, Wong established a new genre of fighting comics with Little Rascals (Siu Lau-man), a realistic story of young people living in the Hong Kong housing projects and fighting gangsters and criminals. The best-selling Little Rascals spawned many imitators; the main competition was Seung-gun Siu-bo's 1971 Lee Siu-lung (Bruce Lee's Chinese name). When the colonial government passed the Indecent Publication Law in 1975, banning explicit violence in comic books, Wong established the daily newspaper Sang Po to continue his work because only comic books, not daily papers, were covered by the law. He also changed the name of Little Rascals to Oriental Heroes (Lung Fu Mun).

By the end of the 1970s, Wong and Seung-gun were the two main artists producing fighting comics. Although several competing manhua newspapers provided venues for younger artists, theywere too inexperienced to be threats to Wong and Seung-gun. In the early 1980s, Seung-gun joined Wong's company, Jademan Comics, consolidating their positions in the marketplace. Their King Manhua Weekly, introduced in 1982, showcased Hong Kong's top manhua artists. Wong also added two martial arts titles to Jademan's catalog, Drunken Master (1982) and Buddha's Palm (1983). Included in the first issues of Drunken Master was another new series, Ma Wing-shing's The Chinese Hero (Chungwah Ying-hung). Ma's realistic style and superb drawings garnered the series immediate recognition; when it appeared separately in 1983, it marked the beginning of a new era in martial arts comics

The success of Wong's Jademan Comics helped establish Hong Kong's manhua business as a major industry. In 1986, the company was listed on the stock market, making it the first publicly traded comics firm. However, when the market crashed in October 1987, Jademan suffered greatly. In the following years, some of the company's most promising artists, including Fung Chi-ming, Lau Ding-gin, Ma Wing-shing, and Seung-gun, left to establish their own firms

In the late 1980s and early 1990s, fighting manhua became more diverse. Some of the outstanding works of the era, such as Tin Ha Pictorial, by Ma Wing-shing, and Dagger, Sword, Laugh, by Fung Chi-ming, upheld the traditions of the genre. However, several new types of fighting comics appeared, including the video game-derived Street Fighter stories, science fiction such as Li Chi-tat's Black Mask, and a gambling series called The Legend of the God of Gamblers. Wong Yuk-long also created a period-costume martial arts series, The Legend of Emperors, in 1993.

Gangster manhua remained popular, although some titles, like Teddy Bøy, introduced in 1992, came close to glorifying violence and vice. As competition between manhua publishers intensified, some works overstepped the acceptable boundaries of violent and sexual content. In response, the Hong Kong government amended the Indecent Publication Law, forcing publishers to seal explicit comics in plastic bags, attach warning notices on the covers, and limit their sale to adult readers. These regulations remain in effect.

# 圖環連子原化俗通部全

![](images/8be2b4afbcae77de2bfcaecf3c6faac50f6f77cdf0d2df7fb4504089342e95ef.jpg)

## 1.

## The Eastward Conquer of Xue Ren-gui, 1920 Art: Liu Bo-liang

This Shanghai comic shows what early martial arts works looked like as evolved from the traditional sequential picture book, or lianhuantu. Chinese line drawings appear on the lower part of the page and descriptive text at the top. There were thirty pages in each issue of the series and sixty issues in the entire volume. This example tells the traditional tale of a national hero of the Sung dynasty, Xue Ren-gui.

## 2-4.

Wu Zong Wrecks Hou's Mansion: Superheroes Lianhuantu Pictorial, 1920s

Art/Script: Unknown

This 1920s series follows the adventures of superheroes in full historical costume, focusing not only on sword fighting but also on traditional Chinese mythology. Published in Shanghai, it ran for twentyfour issues, making up three volumes.

## 5-7.

All Quiet on the Western Front, 1930s

This series, though based on an American movie version of the German novel about the First World War, followed the traditional form of lianhuantu: pictures below and text above. T’he characters are in Western costume but are drawn as Chinese, not Europeans.

## 8–10.

Nan-yang Gate, 1947

Art: Unknown

Like All Quiet on the Western Front, this volume, printed in Shanghai and based on an historical incident during the Tang dynasty, followed the traditional form. Special characters in the lower corner highlight details of the story.

## 11–14.

This lianhuantu first appeared in a daily newspaper. When it was collected into a booklet, it moved away from the traditional format of lianhuantu by including six frames on each page. The cover of Fully Popularized Atomic Lianhuantu uses “atomic” to attract the reader's attention (see illustration 14).

## 15.

Spy Heroes, 1949 Art: Chau Ping Script: Lo Lik

This work is clearly influenced by American action comics. The figures are Westernized with modern costumes, and they engage in cowboylike adventures. The story itself is about a female Chinese spy, Chan Lai, during the Japanese occupation of China.

![](images/ddda514e428fb8eb725276ab9b9e40977f967e385715557a1c5f23a09ace2f44.jpg)

![](images/60234ef3328da3c85743fb2335f549ca4b76a784169310c86ab700b1d3fce6d7.jpg)

![](images/127626d5b29001f3ed8746ff33b046e7d0234ec5123e52b2f1f0c279146880f8.jpg)

![](images/ff43ac00e45683c276386b91823a7ecff5671a504cb89fd363788bb7b345cca2.jpg)

16-36.

Assorted titles, late 1940s Art/Script: Various artists

After the Communists took control of mainland China in 1949, Hong Kong suffered a shortage of popular reading materials. The owners of the city's lianhuantu kiosks——where readers rented the illustrated books for a small fee—needed to find an alternative supply, which they did either by drawing their own or by hiring artists to draw them. In style and format, these lianhuantu closely followed those made in Shanghai before the Second World War; most were based on classical Chinese mythology and historical tales. They feature fighting, war, ghost stories, or sword-fighting epics and they appealed mainly to male readers. The lianhuantu covers were often removed and glued to posters at the rental kiosks to advertise the titles available. As a result, many of the surviving comics from this era are missing their original covers.

42.

![](images/5033642402f87cf3ff3aab74cd8f13e7151f0968497ef9b1e202130a94c45dd8.jpg)  
37.

![](images/b32fd9c15781e3770eebbe6a407ec4e3ded93059ade4767f0ac6811b453a65c8.jpg)

![](images/df0f66e4a755d0079122f6d04c91c07beab73f7f78b3a1759fc80d93a6e19dfb.jpg)

![](images/81dddf4bb0fcdf14ddd2c9fc3c9ebaddc861ab6ca805d3d29cf63f2847e90afd.jpg)  
38.

![](images/971c81cae368455206bb950a5c5b067bb5237b6ec57905c2944325a0671ea6fd.jpg)  
39.

![](images/5c801db66c838411d93ddb8762644c064a81df279059bbb88e6dcdb1d6c7af07.jpg)

![](images/63d246b74322081b112fa14f8bd2f2aa1a55772a77a6c1e95092fa35cfe9815d.jpg)  
40.

![](images/4bc2b27d62196f359d3745999327c4197b951c8a2ff77275f3d50a8c22167b5b.jpg)  
44.

![](images/c8192da0a33107a4bdb2c420f2b75722e7dad4bafbb9d5ba81a43a6a836434dd.jpg)

![](images/38840cf11a4cf5b65d1a937f0a1c2697395dfbd6e525d24efc4a75e944adf621.jpg)

![](images/2e571f2b7dc3a6b0cc4f1de82060e06c49bbf884e587ae5a582367bda41adab1.jpg)

![](images/2c1388d8969ec99b5e5b4617d73da684172431af5f8cb32848b480f2b83ca350.jpg)

![](images/59404de3f5fed65789d184f48bd96d562e5af3733bbe5fedb0460d47aab881b3.jpg)

![](images/b9c4468cdfbbe5f84595a57857b5f1acc512d7b310d77df5b6a29fb04f914092.jpg)

![](images/ba3c5fbccc8b71f3bf8919fc32ad26b39d2b939768e10f324e45b59b9c54468e.jpg)

37–51.

Uncle Choi, 1958

Art/Script: Hui Guan-man

Uncle Choi (Choi Suk) is one of the most memorable characters of Hong Kong manhua. At first, Hui told Choi's story in a humorous manner, but the tone soon became more serious as he focused on the war with Japan and made Choi a war hero. Uncle Choi was the best-selling comic in Hong Kong for several years and continued publishing for more than a decade and a half. When it first appeared, Uncle Choi was considered a creative break from traditional lianhuantu: it used modern fashion, and its story was much closer to real life than those of classic comics. During its long run, its content was adjusted to fit readers’ evolving demands. When James Bond movies and Japanese spy manga became popular, for example, Uncle Choi too became a spy, with machine guns and lasers rather than traditional weapons. However, some of these changes were not well received by readers, and the comic began to decline. It finally ceased in the mid-1970s.

58.

59.

![](images/11e10ff74dcf7164aaa4e362f5a969003fcabec347f134c858994da94d6fe947.jpg)

![](images/0ea7a499bc4aa2d4a69ba864e718a9dccc8cae9bcce71e6a97ecf49a2ccb761e.jpg)

![](images/7d20cf3ce8f6c69fa06c5421cc2032c9e05b22212ef11a31f33b57dd34e9fa37.jpg)

![](images/e1f91306bf3ec8a555ea075495bdf74d2061f8d73ac36784663c5db2b0ea34c4.jpg)  
63.

52-57.

Boy Scout, 1960 Art: Ng Gei-ping

This popular comic follows the adventures of two Boy Scouts, Siu-ming and Siu Sam-ji. However, they are not typical Boy Scouts. Instead, like Uncle Choi, they are war heroes in the fight against Japan. Their adventures included combat, bombings, capturing enemy prisoners, and other similar events. Since the comic used the official name of the Boy Scouts, it was banned at the organization's Hong Kong headquarters. Eventually the Boy Scouts sued to prevent the writer from using the name, and the series title was changed to Siu-ming.

58–63.

Flying Black Batman, 1962 Art: Ho Yat-kwan

The late 1950s and early 1960s saw many locally produced science-fiction adventure comics. Most of these superhero manhua were directly influenced by American action comics, and it was common to see exact copies of Superman, Batman, Spiderman, and other American characters. When it first came out, Flying Black Batman was the number-two best-seller, following Uncle Choi. Other titles by the same artist include Clean-face Conqueror, Super Black Conqueror, Atomic Seven, and King Detective.

![](images/bca7c24cf6c763b87c4fff9f9a538567cb0b99f1a03f8e4ac1a85bde96db8f7a.jpg)

## 64-65.

Movie Martial Arts Novel Manhua, 1966 Art: Various artists

The popular sword-fighting films of the 1960s inspired many comics with similar themes, including Reflective Cave, by Ng Gei-ping, and The Killing Order ofMo-lum, by Ho Yat-kwan. These particular titles were simply comic-book versions of the films.

## 66-75.

Assorted titles, 1966–1972

Art/Script: Various artists

In the late 1960s and early 1970s, the most popular manhua were published as five-by-seven-inch comic books that cost twenty cents each. They covered a wide range of themes and subjects, from science fiction, romance, and kung fu to humor, soft porn, and ghost stories. The science-fiction stories were often direct copies or imitations of American comics, although some were technological updates of traditional Chinese stories. Romance manhua dealt with the traditional issues of love stories, such as betrayal and suffering. Soft-porn comics walked a thin line between sex and censorship, although some titles included more explicit images of women and sex on their covers than others.

## 76-77.

Science Fiction Series, 1966–1974

Art/Script: Dung Fong-yung

This series of science-fiction manhua illustrates the influence and eventual integration of American comics and Japanese manga. Science Wonder Boy is an imitation of a Japanese manga, Tetsujin 28, by Mitsuteru Yokoyama, and Black Batman features the hero and all of the villains of the American Batman stories.

## 78–80.

Lies of the Journey to the West, 1976 Art/Script: Tsui Yu-on

In the face of the strong influence of American action comics and Japanese manga, even traditional Chinese stories had to be modernized in order to compete. This title was based on the traditional story of The Monkey King, The Buddhist Monk Tung Sam, and His Fellows. Another popular title by the same artist was Electric Pig, which follows the adventures of a pig with superpowers——he could resist lasers and other weapons——who helped fight criminals.

100.

![](images/202db8b2f0f0aef634be4c8c2e329818a01d1810553170e66290d48139d4bf71.jpg)  
101.

Little Vagabond, 1968   
Art/Script: Wong Yuk-long   
Little Vagabond, one of Wong Yuk-long's earliest comics, follows the humorous action adventures of a drunken Chinese god, Vagabond.

## 82.

The Son of Ultraman, 1969 Art/Script: Wong Yuk-long

The great popularity of pirated Japanese manga inspired many local imitations of the style, especially of the manga hero Ultraman. Wong's version, which stayed very close to the original's story and style, is the best of these copies.

## 83.

Solar Lord, 1969

Art/Script: Wong Yuk-long

Solar Lord, an original creation of Wong Yuk-long, features battles between good and evil in a futuristic setting. The action and drawing style in this comic became Wong's signature; they are also seen in his best-selling comic Little Rascals.

84-101.

Little Rascals, 1970

Art/Script: Wong Yuk-long

Wong Yuk-lung's most popular comic, Little Rascals, was translated literally to English from Siu Lau-man, and carried connotations of a little gangster with antisocial behavior. It was an original creation about a group of unemployed young hoodlums living in Hong Kong's public housing. The story revolves around Wong Siu-fu and his elder brother, Wong Siulung, whose one hair-covered eye brands him as “bad." He turns away from his criminal behavior, however, to help Wong Siu-fu fight evil. Little Rascals's stories emphasize brotherhood, and although the brothers are fighting, they are fighting for justice.

In the first issues, the fighting often involved chains, which led to spilled blood. Knives (and ropes, clubs, and daggers) appeared in later issues, and the enemies’ internal organs, guts, and bones were seen, often quite graphically. Little Rascals quickly acquired a large following, and although it had a positive message, its main attraction was violent action. Within a few years of its first appearance, many people in Hong Kong were criticizing the violence in Little Rascals and other fighting comics. The Indecent Publication Law of 1975 called for less-graphic violence. Depictions ofbloodshed remained common, but in response to the pressure, Wong changed the title to a more positive name, Oriental Heroes (Lung Fu Mun) and experimented with other venues.

![](images/9c72adc5d51241701e0c4aace33e72f97fa3a9f3950ddc87097c01a96783e27e.jpg)  
119.

102–103.

Sang Po, 1975

Publisher: Wong Chun-lung (Wong Yuk-long)

Sang Po was Wong's first daily publication. At first, the tabloid-size newspaper tried to imitate regular papers, covering entertainment, health, and news and featuring columns by famous writers such as James Wong, Sha Yung, Tong Mo-lee, and Fung Ka. Comics filled only three-and-a-half pages each day, with Wong's Oriental Heroes appearing in full color on the back cover. After a month, Sang Po switched to pure manhua.

## 104–119.

Oriental Heroes, 1975

Art/Script: Wong Yuk-long

When Wong gave the Little Rascals series its new title, he made a few other changes: the setting was moved to Japan; he printed a note saying that the story was fictional; and he explored ways to depict violence without explicit bloodshed. He relied on fire, water, poison, and other methods of violence. Wong's drawing technique also improved, especially in his depiction of muscles and the lines denoting action. Oriental Heroes was published every day in Sang Po and collected each week in book form.

In the mid-1980s, Wong modified his drawing style again, making it more serious and less comical in response to the success of Ma Wing-shing's realistic The Chinese Hero. After 1991, Oriental Heroes was drawn by other artists; it is still published today, with a total run of more than 1,000 issues.

![](images/4b8f35eac953275c72eed92ec578800c7740f99abf64986267c67c321f66386a.jpg)

![](images/c08961d4610071897a0e54be3e709aeeed20da75375d146d8006669718ac6314.jpg)

![](images/11d00c460fd9b542a2c79594ee3dd37b696e7082ea13553356a5b18678fee717.jpg)

![](images/d7b5107c43ebd627eb7f1bcb22eb8835821ec0b345037b6789336e008318ce8a.jpg)  
135.

![](images/483ebdd9f26bc46e62ea3c3f2c4a75734505e7e9ed9e5e218d84c5320fbf120b.jpg)

![](images/204d500571346c3b32c0b6154b0199448cd285c26d0ea8bbe5f0dd16a7eaba2e.jpg)  
136.

## 120-134.

Lee Siu-lung (Bruce Lee), 1971

Art/Script: Seung-gun Siu-bo

This comic, which used Bruce Lee's Chinese name as its title, emphasizes the use of martial arts to fight against evil and for justice. The main character, Lee Siu-lung, was drawn with physical features similar to those of the real Bruce Lee. Other characters included Lee's follower, Sa-dam Chai, a young rebel with strong principles. Violent acts such as eye gouging were depicted graphically, although less explicitly than in Wong's Little Rascals. Still, after the 1975 restrictions were imposed, the artist was forced to publish the comic daily in the newspaper Hei Bo to maintain its audience.

135–136.

Hei Bo, 1975

Publisher: Seung-gun Siu-bo

Entertainment news covered the front page of this newspaper; the remaining pages were filled with manhua. The manhua features were Lee Siu-lung, on the inside front page, and Sheng-gun Siu-wai's Brother of Dragon and Tiger, printed in full color on the back page. The newspaper lasted only two years, folding in early 1977.

## 137–138.

## Ching Bo, 1977

Publisher: Seung-gun Siu-bo

The name of this newspaper, which means “green," conveyed its youthful and energetic spirit, as most of its manhua artists—including Wong Kwok-hing, Fung Chi-ming, Ma Wing-shing, Tse Chi-wing, and Chiu Lui-tak—were less than twenty years old. Ma Wing-shing's work Experiment of the Devil showed particularly great potential.

## 139–144.

Golden Bo Daily, 1977

## Publisher: Wong Chun-lung

The main attraction of this manhua newspaper was Wong Yuk-long's Oriental Heroes. Other featured series were Sau Sing-chai, by Sheng-gun Siu-keung, Ghost Story, by Hon Man, and Ma Wing-shing's The Blood Sword, which first appeared in Golden Bo’s pages. These outstanding artists made Golden Bo the best-selling daily manhua newspaper, beating out its sister publicatión, Sang Po. Both papers were published until the late 1980s.

## 145.

Charming, 1978

Art/Script: Ma Wing-shing

This early work by Ma Wing-shing shows signs of his trademark shadows, smoothly drawn figures, and realistic backgrounds. The story line, which combines traditionally costumed characters with modern ones, promotes justice.

158.

## 146-148.

Lianhuan Daily, 1981

Art: Various artists

This full-color newspaper's main attractions were Little Bar Girl, by Sheng-gwan Siu-wai, Lee Siu-lung, by Seung-gun Siu-bo, and Iron Dragon Man, by Fung Chi-ming.

## 149.

Tin Lung Comics, 1982

Art: Various artists

This daily newspaper featured sword-fighting epic Tiger Panther Heroes on the front and Shui Mukleung's The Dragon God's Battle on the back. As the manhua newspaper industry boomed in the 1980s, many newcomers entered the field, but some older manhua artists also wanted to try out the popular format, to test their commercial viability. Tin Lung Comics never became very popular; the stories and drawing style of the older generation did not suit the contemporary readers’ tastes. Tin Lung Comics marked the beginning of a downward trend for daily manhua papers.

## 150–151.

## King Manhua Weekly, 1982

As the comics industry boomed in the 1980s, it was led by the manhua empire of Wong Yuk-long. King Manhua Weekly began publishing shortly after Wong hired some of his key competitors, including Seunggun Siu-bo and Seung-gun Siu-keung. Together with the outstanding artists already on Wong's staff these new contributors created top-quality comics; the tabloid-size, sixteen-page magazine served as a showcase for the company. Redrawn versions of some of Wong's early works—Little Vagabond and Solar Lord—also appeared in the weekly. Although the comic feature on the magazine's cover was usually a space adventure, a variety of action, adventure, and humorous comics appeared inside.

## 152–156.

Drunken Master, 1982

Art/Script: Wong Yuk-long

Although the title of this comic comes from Jackie Chan's 1978 movie of the same name, the story did not follow the film. Instead, it tells the story of a young man who fights off unsavory characters as he learns martial arts. Drunken Master was also produced as a television series in 1984.

157–158.

Buddha's Palm, 1982

Art/Script: Wong Yuk-long

The first seventy issues of this costumed comic were based on Jin Yong's sword-fighting epic, Story of the Sword and the Sabre. The main character is Lung Kimfei, a young man who is given the chance to become the leader of the Mo-lum martial arts society.

# 報海雄英華中

![](images/ac5e45a11b4db96efd01cf063510c9497ed5fd17cb7c97122e8d0988a39ca5e9.jpg)

170.

![](images/82b2ea0f5e12ee710f6a8cc0f5c3f17c261912c37ed00e33f076fb544e8c4eec.jpg)

159–171.

The Chinese Hero (Chung-wah Ying-hung), 1983 Art/Script: Ma Wing-shing

When The Chinese Hero first appeared——as a supplement to Wong Yuk-long's Drunken Master, then as its own comic book—it made the artist, Ma Wing-shing, something of an instant legend, in part because of his rags-to-riches life story, but mainly because of the skill and style of the work. Ma's comic, which was considered a breakthrough at the time, had a realistic style, clear and detailed action scenes, engrossing plots, and skillful use of color. Ma's style was influenced by the Japanese manga Crying Killer, by the artist Ryoichi Ikegami.

The Chinese Hero tells the story of Wah Yinghung, a young man living happily with his parents and fiancée until one tragic night: a man murders his parents and tries to kill him, too. Both of his parents were experts in martial arts, and he inherited their skills. He escapes, marries his fiancée, and they have twins; eventually the story shifts its focus to Wah Yinghung and his children's fight against evil.

When The Chinese Hero appeared as a standalone comic, it was an immediate hit, selling some 45,000 copies. Over the next several years, the title's circulation averaged 60,000 and hit peaks of 200,000. Ma became the hottest manhua artist in Hong Kong, and the English version, The Blood Sword (later renamed Blood Sword Dynasty) was published and distributed in North America. In 1989, Ma left Wong's Jademan Comics; however, the company owned the title and continued to publish it under the direction of another artist, Yuan Ga-bo. This comic became a movie in 1999, called A Man Called Hero.

![](images/9c8e355495de9e0d4bb7ae4e20dc457d4df1353ec5cb35aa325a9ee7be28626b.jpg)

## 172-176.

![](images/ad1bcef6755f9efd34720558881400c686429f9e569a247d9ece758f66f74d85.jpg)  
184.

Dagger, Sword, Laugh, 1988

Art: Fung Chi-ming

Script: Lau Ding-gin

## 177-187.

## Tin Ha Pictorial, 1989

![](images/c9b718222a9f3cfcde8203d1e06b6b2acad2119f2ab053a06e486d85e8399f1f.jpg)  
181.  
185.  
182.

This comic was drawn by an up-and-coming artist of the time, Fung Chi-ming. The martial arts story deals with themes of good against evil and revenge. Although the plot is not very original, it is still strong, and Fung's drawings are very detailed, with appealing figures and experimental coloring.

Art/Script: Ma Wing-shing with assistant, Siu Kit Tin Ha Pictorial was the first manhua put out by Ma’s own company, Jonsky Publishing. Issues one through four contain the story of the North and South Poles, which were followed by Wind and Cloud. Ma experimented with color technique and composition, using several different mediums: air brush, watercolor, and colored pencils. In 1998, the manhua was made into a movie, The Storm Rider.

![](images/088969b3ca83e68ba2cc51a4e80048a132b6ded7320b84a2b746a485a4435116.jpg)  
197.

## 188.

Bat-hoi Bun: The Second Short Works by Li Chi-tat, 1990

Art/Script: Li Chi-tat

This is a collection of the artist's work from 1987 to 1990 and includes seven short stories. Li's work was greatly influenced by the Japanese manga artist Katsuhiro Otomo, who experimented with shadow, realistic and detailed drawings, and space and composition. The comic's title refers to the technical term for its size, bat-hoi bun, which is approximately ten by fourteen inches.

## 189.

The King of Street Fighter, 1991

Art: Hui King-sam

Script: Lee Chung-hing

The main characters of this comic——Boxer Man and the Chinese Kung Fu Man—were taken from the popular video game Street Fighter. The artist's outstanding drawing technique and the popularity of the game made the title a quick best-seller. Its success helped start the industry trend of basing comics on video-game titles, characters, and stories.

## 190.

Steel Warrior Adventures, 1991

Art/Script: Khoo Fuk-lung

This manhua is a typical sword-fighting epic, which details the main character's encounters with evil and his fight for power, fortune, and treasure.

## 191–194.

Black Leopard, 1991

Art: Wan Yat-leung

Script: Tang Chi-fai

This title is a typical kung fu gangster manhua set in Hong Kong: the main character is a legendary man fighting against evil. Wan was one of the rising comic stars after Ma Wing-shing, and this work was produced while he worked at Ma's company. It was later drawn by Ma's assistant, Siu Kit.

## 195.

Street Warrior, 1991

Art/Script: Chi Man

Street Warrior was one of the many imitators of the successful The King of Street Fighter. It too revolves around video game-style street fighting, although it also contains elements of science fiction.

## 196–197.

The Legend of the God of Gambler, 1991

Art: Sze-to Gim-kiu and Wing Yan

This is a manhua version of the hit action movie God of Gambler, which stars Chow Yun-fat. The movie started a sub-genre of not only Hong Kong film, but also of manhua. Other examples of this style include Youth God ofGamblers and The Unbeatable Con Man.

![](images/491ae2d08d95b44ec4971373285ccac4075ddc8cbce16432428de41ca9900614.jpg)

![](images/0f7ca6cc339f7aed9b3d9cb52ae5b19e1e5e2ec3f9ec753c8690f0b48b22028a.jpg)

198-199.

Black Mask, 1992 Art: Li Chi-tat Script: Pang Chi-ming

This manhua was an experimental combination of mainstream and innovative drawing styles, such as the use of movie camera-style points of view. 'The story itself follows a skilled martial arts man in a large city. During the day he is an ordinary person, but at night he dons a leather mask and goes out to help the city's poor and helpless. In 1996, the comic was made into a movie starring Jet Li.

200–203.

Teddy Boy, 1992

Art: Lun Yu-kwok

Script: Man Kam-hung

Teddy Boy was the first comic in the triad gangster fighting genre, which evolved from sword-fighting epics, kung fu comics, and the styles of Wong Yuklung and Ma Wing-shing. The story is violent, with graphic blood and gore. The genre includes such comics as Portland Street, Teddy Boy 1/2, and Kong-wu Big Brother, most of which carried warnings, were sealed in plastic bags, and could be sold only to people older than eighteen.

## 204.

The Dragon in Prison, 1993

Art: Seung-gun Siu-bo and Ow Man

The Dragon in Prison is a story of life in prison; its main character is an inmate with intricate tattoos of dragons. The comic was published about the time Wong Yuk-long was released from prison, and a rumor spread that the source of the story was Wong's own experience behind bars. In fact, the plot had nothing to do with Wong; it is just another gangster story.

205–206.

The Legend of Emperors, 1993 Art: Wong Yuk-long and Khoo Fuk-lung Script: Wong Yik

In 1991, Wong Yuk-long was convicted of fraud for covering up the financial failings of his company, Jademan Comics. He served two years in prison; The Legend of Emperors was his first comic after his release, and was published by his new company, Jade Dynasty. It is an action manhua based on mythological events set in the Shang dynasty. A sequel set in the Qin dynasty came out in 1996, and a third volume, set during the Han dynasty, was published in 1998.

## 207.

The Assassination of Emperor Qin, 1996 Art/Script: Li Chi-tat

As in his earlier work, Black Mask, Li Chi-tat experimented in this comic with new compositions and points of view. The story is a retelling of the wellknown tale of the assassin of Emperor Qin. The story was rendered with a fresh, new perspective.

![](images/360116b3c7bee95273b926c76277e5c8a6fe737a66a0322655aff5bd127d91ad.jpg)

![](images/9d00c1c9d098c998883b48ccb5983eb7105511fd08de526233027465c1d0f5b3.jpg)

## 208.

Strong Force, 1994

Art/Script: Xu De-sheng

Xu De-sheng is a Taiwanese comic artist famous for realistic color drawings. When this work, with its eighty color panels, was released in 1994, the technique attracted a great deal of attention. However, his innovative technique did not translate into success in the marketplace.

## 209.

## Double-faced Man, 1994

Art/Script: Wong Chun-kei and Hau Chung-dong With money they won in a local manhua competition, Wong Chun-kei and Hau Chung-dong created the first Hong Kong comic produced, in part, with computers. The three-dimensional backgrounds in the book were produced digitally, and the artists experimented with digital effects on hand-drawn art.

210–217.

Sea Tiger I–III, 1994

Art: Tang Chi-fai

Editor: Wan Yat-leung

Tang and Wan's Sea Tiger, considered one of the classic manhua of the 1990s, combines two genres: fighting and science fiction. The comic tells the story of a young man (nicknamed Sea Tiger) whose father was killed by the Dream Blue Organization. Over the course ofits three-year 144-issue run, readers followed his often violent battles with that organization, as well as romantic subplots.

![](images/0d9240418d16c5b61118e1641f916bd642115f78cc163a83cc43f2a810b17e11.jpg)

At the beginning of the twentieth century, children in Hong Kong could choose to read two kinds of things outside of school: illustrated stories that appeared in the local newspapers, either as columns or supplements, or illustrated story books imported from Shanghai. Before the Second World War, Shanghai was much more progressive than Hong Kong; in fact, it is considered the first modern city in China. Many of the cultural advances that came to China in the twentieth century appeared in Shanghai first. The first Chinese-language kids' magazine, Enlightenment Pictorial, for example, was published there in 1908. Compared to that of Shanghai, Hong Kong's media suffered from a lack of talent; the first locally produced children's magazine, New Children's Bimonthly Magazine, didn't appear until 1941. It was the most influential children's periodical in Hong Kong until the early 1950s, when the influx of refugees from mainland China increased demand for all kinds of reading materials. Unfortunately, the shortage of talent continued to affect the quality of local magazines.

Hong Kong's daily newspapers, including Wah Kiu Daily and Sing Tao Daily, began to produce original material geared toward young readers. Some small publishers also published picture books for kids, although most were reprinted prewar magazines or pirated Chinese-language versions of Western books and Disney cartoons. Palm-size picture books produced before the war—known as lianhuanhua shu or kungchai shu—were also available for rent from street kiosks. Until the 1950s, these were essentially the only sources for children's comics.

The appearance of Children's Paradise in 1953, edited by Law Guan-tsiu, changed all that and marked the beginning of the children's manhua industry in Hong Kong. The magazine's success not only inspired imitators, but it also helped nurture local manhua talent, which fueled the comics boom of the 1950s and 1960s. Television was not broadcast in Hong Kong, so reading was one of the main forms of entertainment for the city’s children. In the 1950s and 1960s, too, many public and private libraries were founded, along with public housing projects equipped with reading rooms, making children's reading materials more widely available.

After television was introduced in 1967, the production of local children's comics began to drop off. Japanese manga became more popular—a by-product in part of the Chinese-dubbed Japanese animation that appeared on TV. Pirated editions of manga soon appeared; Fujio Fujiko's Doraemon, published in Japan in 1970, was redrawn and printed without permission as Ding Dong in Children’'s Paradise in the mid-1970s. Television also inspired several non-manga comics, including Children's TV (1975), Happy Together Children’s Pictorial (1975), and Big Brother Sunny (1978).

In the 1980s and 1990s, Hong Kong's children's magazines struggled to stay afloat. The two longest-lived titles, Children's Paradise and Little Friends Pictorial, both folded in the 1990s. Several new magazines did emerge, such as Sing Tao Good Children’s Pictorial (1982), as did a short-lived children's newspaper, Children's Daily (1989), and some authorized Disney magazines. But kids’ comics have never again been as popular as they were in the 1950s and 1960s. In order to survive, publishers had to find ways of making money. Many of the original children's comics from this era, such as Little Ming Pao Weekly (1989), Whiz-Kids Express Weekly (1993), and Yellow Bus (1990), appeared as supplements to weekly publications. Of these, Yellow Bus was the most successful, although at the cost of almost constant adaptation to appeal to young readers’ shifting tastes.

Today, the market for children's comics in Hong Kong is dominated by Japanese manga; very little material is produced locally. Taking a wider view of the genre, one sees that kids’ reading materials were constantly influenced by sources outside Hong Kong: first by Shanghai manhua, then by American comics, and more recently by Japanese manga and anime. The brief era when original creations such as Children’s Paradise and Little Friends Pictorial dominated the market is now considered the golden age of Hong Kong children's manhua

## ？麽什到得裹煤從們我 李

大家都知辽划是一种燃科，它可以轮我们热和力。可是它的功用建多济呢！投不完全的扶计，我们從媒爽面投业出来的真西可以做战八十多视的物買像乾宏池，船纸心·自来蛋水筆電站，智聲典叫碟，玻璃雨友，玻璃抹葚，英民透刚牙擦，拍油味精戴梢香朴·臣士匹重，咳榮高夷救虫藥·炸藥毒樂，颜科，油漆，亞躁尼亞，肥田料等等日常所見的東西共所用的醇料，或者是直接类媒提炖出来，或者用提块出来的物贵优合其他原料做成的。下面的圆不過是少数的我很例子强了。

## 1.

Children's Pictorials Bimonthly Magazine, 1923 This was one of the first children's magazines in China. Although it was published in Shanghai, it circulated in Hong Kong as well. Its content was mainly illustrations, but jokes, stories, activities, and games were also regular features.

## 2.

## Children's Magazine, 1932

Many early children's magazines published in Shanghai were circulated all over China in the 1930s. This type of magazine was the first example in the modern history of China of a magazine aimed at providing a well-rounded education outside the classroom.

## 3-6.

## Children's World Bimonthly, 1932

During the Japanese occupation and the Second World War, this long-running magazine moved from Shanghai to Changsha, and its content reflected wartime priorities. General information about the conflict was printed, as well as features on preserving country and family. Over a long career, Children's World Bimonthly included a more diverse array of content than previous children's magazines.

## 7-14.

## Young Children's Literature Collection, 1934

This Shanghai-based series included both text and illustrations covering health, society, and natural science, as well as arts and crafts projects and quizzes. Also featured were stories that included historical Chinese tales, Western fairy tales, and stories of everyday life. It lasted for more than 200 issues.

## 15.

## Good Children's Weekly: Children's Reading Materials, 1938

This magazine, begun at the outbreak of the Sino-Japanese War, was filled with anti-Japanese propaganda. Its purpose was to promote nationalism, and it urged children to support the war and eventually fight it, with cover stories such as “Children of China, Stand Up” and “I Want to Go Too, Papa.”

## 16-18.

## New Children's Bimonthly Magazine, 1941

This was Hong Kong's first literary variety magazine for children. Èdited by Wong Hing-wan and supported by university professors, it included work by such contemporary writers as Xu Di-shan, Fung Zikai, Yan Wen-jing, and Hu Bing-chun. After Hong Kong was occupied by Japan, the editorial staff moved to southern China, where they continued producing the magazine until 1944. The magazine returned to Hong Kong in 1946, where it published fifty more issues. In 1951, it moved back to China; it was renamed Young Pioneers in 1956.

## 19–24.

## World Famous Children's Book Series, 1951

This series of comic books borrowed heavily from Western children's stories, with titles such as Pinocchio, Dance Performance, and The Lone Ranger. In some cases the comics’ English name was used while others were translated into Chinese, as in Tsam-gao Adventures. Pinocchio's English name was removed completely; the Chinese version was titled Muk-nau Kiyu Kee (Wooden Puppet Adventures). However, the story and drawings closely resemble the original.

## 25.

## Wah Kiu Daily Supplement, 1951

After the Communists took control of mainland China, a great wave of immigrants hit Hong Kong. In response to the needs of a growing population (and it's children), some daily newspapers added columns and supplements geared toward kids. This column appeared in Wah Kiu Daily as a Sunday supplement. It featured fairy tales, stories about science, quizzes, and comic strips.

## 26.

## Children's Healthy Series Literature, 1952

In the early 1950s there were few talented local comics artists, and many Hong Kong comic books were simply direct copies of American examples. Many were reprints of the original, with the text translated into Chinese and references changed to local names and places. In other cases, American comics were redrawn. This comic book, The Christmas Gift, features a redrawn version of Felix the Cat. Neither the reprinted nor the redrawn comics give credit to the original sources.

## 27.

The Children's Educational Picture Book Collection, 1952

While many manhua publishers used American cartoons as their source, this series instead copied illustrated Western fairy tales. The collection included The Phantom of the Opera, The Snowman's Winter, Little Paradise, and Little Penguin Travels to the South. Although based on Western sources, readers were seldom informed of the original English titles; sometimes, local artists or editors received credit. These kinds of comic books were priced low enough for kids to buy them with candy money.

## 28-29.

## The Blessed Young Children Magazine, 1952

This magazine for elementary-school children was put out by a Christian educational publisher. It mainly printed Christian stories, particularly from the Bible, but also included nonreligious stories intended to promote morality. The magazine contained more text than most comic books; the pictures functioned primarily to support the story, reflecting the editorial bias toward the benefits of reading.

## ⑤友朋小

![](images/96f8a21b90a0f08f65a2486ab1f778cce2bdf2167e980c896d60f82560cbe469.jpg)  
34.  
35.

## 30友朋小

![](images/36ef3107202e64132f0ea16fa4d844100243a15c4e45dbddc58082d171051260.jpg)

![](images/039080862b424814f2b9d608ed9ac69702a2761b45fb7b9d6c1b1370f671c638.jpg)

![](images/ffd32e37b16286601fc8fbd268c5c366f479b3b6840a0a471daba0496c5480b5.jpg)

![](images/8b3236f3fae8275a3784e65c6fa9cd56e5333219acbf3b89d6d9d1e4126d08c8.jpg)  
38.

用友小

![](images/47e19884037c3eb4fdac8b6e9f48e04bb8dcd72f5c1caf428e4dce5184f1efb4.jpg)

![](images/cc8dd6ad7dbbcac25bfa52ae69579d3e76974b3cdc114759e985f84f4e124c80.jpg)

Like The Blessed Young Children Magazine, this magazine for middle school children also printed more text than pictures; its content focused on helping and educating kids as they entered their teenage years. Printed in Hong Kong, it was distributed in Chinese communities all over Southeast Asia.

## 31–32.

## Children's Knowledge Educational Series, 1953

This series was drawn and edited by a famous child psychiatrist, Chung Chun-wing, who wanted to give Hong Kong's children healthy after-school reading material. Most of the characters were copied from American sources, such as these versions of Bugs Bunny and Elmer Fudd. Chung's work and other Westernbased comics led to the widespread assimilation of American styles by Hong Kong manhua artists.

## 33.

## Sing Tao Daily Pictorial Supplement, 1953

Every Sunday, the Sing Tao Daily issued an eightpage color supplement that consisted mainly of children's comics. Its main contributors included Bao Dik-cheung, Lee Ling-hon, Chang Yat-yim, and Mak Ching. In 1957, Bao became the supplement's editor and changed its format to a full regular-newspaper page, rather than a tabloid page. In the larger size, only four pages were published. Although some American comics were included, most were Chinese, including Bao's works Girl with Big Ponytail, So-chau Girl, and Bobby, as well as Ng Gei-ping's Legend of Heroes from Three Dynasties.

## 34–40.

## Little Friend Bimonthly, 1953

This magazine focused on literature and natural science and held regular writing competitions. In 1956, it began to run color cartoons and illustrated stories; titles included Lam Siu-tin, by Ho Kwok-lin, and Fairy Tale Comics, by Liao Bing-xiong. The magazine's first sixty-four covers were color photos provided by readers; later covers were illustrated.

![](images/0f98893889d94fc0b4654d01061d63ae61ab172914e1e3751b0001c3924be31c.jpg)

![](images/0591481e1cc1b712ad8e2fb40b2a04d89a30a93b174b26712e8c2206a8029820.jpg)

園樂童兒

![](images/eb173d9c2ee1cff0b5eeb15c9b04844072d0b7a2aaee8ab80a9c6b3f551d8368.jpg)

![](images/e8deff7e48a94b6a9ba537800fedb6d5092f3cbb09164c037817c44b7a06808e.jpg)

46.

![](images/8ed8ff35ebcf07c896476c7a4c2ae613b3ddf7891bdb0e4a46c64de62a8abd11.jpg)

![](images/8f7432690a205fa08027bc1edb615ae3944aa8d498b58bc6f5254c7556e9b53e.jpg)

![](images/a4908022b9f565a2465a2da39393f1dec6984f0c6fadb61bb1735df01aabbf6f.jpg)

![](images/c93d15b8fb7cea944eafbe1292657eae13afc3371502f5c65be7e5d1e9972786.jpg)

![](images/7e5ab4bd84c4fac1dab17e4bd1a11c81ff7334069db61a5d16dd861b593994ba.jpg)  
49.

41–58.

Children's Paradise, 1953

The first full-color magazine for children published in Hong Kong, Children’s Paradise ran for more than forty years, totaling 1,006 issues. Among the classic manhua characters created in Children's Paradise were Little Roundy, Big Ears, and Red Indian. The magazine also helped introduce manga to Hong Kong when it began running unauthorized copies of the Japanese comic Doraemon in the 1970s.

The success of Children's Paradise was largely due to the leadership of its editor, Law Guan-tsiu. He made sure that the magazine's drawings were always beautiful and appealing to children and that its stories were interesting. The content changed over the years, but the basic form and many of its features remained constant. Some of the perennial favorites were poems, quizzes, historical and mythological stories fairy tales, songs, and “living manhua." The center pages always featured colorful manhua. Many other Hong Kong children's magazines tried to copy the format and features of Children’s Paradise.

## 59-68.

## Little Angeli, 1954

The weekly comic book Little Angeli was very much influenced by the style of American comics. The story revolves around a girl, Little Angeli, who has straight hair and a passion for justice, and her classmates Bobby Fatty, Bookworm William, Peter, and Crying Mary. Little Angeli was also notable for its double covers, front and back, and its inclusion of both Chinese and English text. It was one of four comics published by East Comics and drawn by the three Bao brothers, Bao Wun-cheung, Bao Ching-cheung, and Bao Dikcheung. Their other titles were Big Head Boy, Jungle Prince, and Big Sister Wang.

## 前文撮要

海盗托力在半年前因裙整性热救了王子而结下了一段灰推过次回承湿首尼刺之命，携间坚键挫物B地重逝，再来造烟与原，并有告卜同行，告卜知道兑制的隆院待封王子到图不划，由於他爱小孩于的真城离古。他何托力限利。

告卜講连用蛛挺生的该事.王子.到忙於拈传他们.并同歌渡空挺.联群也来了蓝情热開，海面的温抬也积波空键，盗首龙影一进你油，一遭用卫连钝望暑马上的情料.

79.

![](images/8e1ef0a411d37390672c93402e76927ff0308d929d8cff8f67b6854a9a319e1b.jpg)

## 69–71.

Little Bobo, 1954

The target readers of Little Bobo were young children. The drawing style and cover illustrations were especially cute; the stories were wholesome and usually included a special feature, Story Time by Big Sister Wan (Wan was the editor of the comic). In 1959, Little Angeli and Little Bobo were combined into one comic, Youth Pictorial.

## 72-74.

Jungle Prince, 1954

This series was written primarily for boys and featured jungle adventure stories similar to that of Tarzan. The comic book itself used both front and back covers for comics; often, a second supplementary comic was featured on the back. These occasional titles included UU-Jet, Little Iron Man, and Rocket Man.

## 75–80.

Children's Lok-fung Bao, 1955

This magazine was read mainly by Catholic school students since it was one of the few comics approved and assigned by the schools. During the 1950s, most ofit consisted of translated American stories, although local content appeared later. It also included games, religion-oriented activities, information about topics such as botany and stamp collecting, and morality stories. In the 1960s, its main draw was the continuing story of The Life of the Members of the Lok-fung Club, as well as vivid comics drawn by Wong Chak, Cheung Hon-ming, Ng Ho-cheung, and others. Originally an eight-page, tabloid-size biweekly, it switched in the 1960s to booklet size; in the 1980s, it switched again, to a horizontal orientation, although it was still a booklet. With this last change, all of the the religious content was removed, and it became much like other children's illustrated magazines.

86.
