# 世界兒童 畫報

'Children's Corner

![](images/db64b066ebfca1184de44fbd55e65b79920a38da525c0990367365d27d9b3726.jpg)

![](images/b5ed810139a40ff3f07883e917e638e95a8c4211bd4a6aa0e2be7bc3857379fb.jpg)

![](images/0504663c4fa3a6428c4d244f4c9e5e56a9c8db38d5e6d61b5f44d2aaaf218b33.jpg)

![](images/7c623f73c5833267893deab6228de4ce6b4508cbb1d8c23b16bfcef702c7e849.jpg)

## 81.

The Voice of Good Companion Pictorial, 1956 This was another illustrated magazine assigned by Hong Kong Catholic schools. At first, it was printed in one color and was mostly text, for after-school reading. It later became a full-color illustrated book with less text and less focus on religion.

## 82-85.

## Children's Literary Collection, 1956

Published by Children's Paradise, these children's books were mostly text, but they also included many illustrations. Targeted at students in the fourth and fifth grades, it included stories from famous literature and fairy tales, as well as some original stories and historical tales.

## 86–87.

Silver Light Daily Sunday Color Pictorial, 1956 Silver Light was the first daily produced with offset color printing; every Sunday it published a cartoon supplement edited by the Bao brothers. Featured comics included the Bao’s Little Angeli and Bobby, as well as American comics such as Walt Disney's Mickey Mouse and Donald Duck.

## 88–91.

## Children's Corner Pictorial Fortnightly, 1956

This imitation of Children's Paradise, published by World Publisher, was one of the original's main competitors, although it never achieved the same level of success. In 1978, the magazine was redesigned, and it adopted the name Little Pilots (Fei-gei Chai) Organization from a popular children’s television program. The magazine tried to adapt to kids’ fickle tastes, but the changes were not successful, and it folded soon after the redesign.

92.

95.

106.

![](images/3b124395cdf5ff85dbc3d54a9215a265c346443c0f7263d4e4beb06f760fe1a3.jpg)

## 92.

## Sing Tao Night Daily Pictorial Supplement, 1957

This Sunday newspaper supplement published translated American comics, work by Chan Chi-dor, Lee Ling-hon, Kwan Shan-mei, and Ding Gong, and a column, Good Children, by Wu Sin, who later headed the Sing Tao Newspaper Corporation. She used the name Big Sister Lily to relate to her readers and established a kid's club, the Good Children Sing Tao Club.

## 93.

## Youth Paradise Bimonthly, 1957

This half-illustrated, half-text variety magazine was targeted at teenagers and addressed topics of interest to more mature children. The cover and children's song section were drawn by the editor of Children's Paradise, Law Guan-tsiu.

## 94–97.

## Friends of Children's Bimonthly, 1957

This publication, edited by Cheung Yat-keui, one of the creators of Children’s Paradise, was targeted at elementary school children. Its contents were quite similar to that magazine's, although its drawings were somewhat fess appealing.

## 98–99.

## Little Companion Pictorial, 1960

One of the many imitators of Children's Paradise, Little Companion was aimed at kids in the middle elementary grades. Writers included Wu Shu-yu, whose Little Dumb Elephant was clearly influenced by Disney's Dumbo. Other contributors included Tang Chik-kin, Wong Chak, and Chi Sing.

## 100–106.

## Children's Weekly, 1960

Published every Saturday, this was the first full-color, tabloid-size children's newspaper in Hong Kong. Weekly features included news, art, stories, letters to the editor, comics, games, and stories contributed by readers. In addition to comic strips and stories, it published interviews with working mothers and their children. Children's Weekly was a quick best-seller, reaching a circulation of6,000 to 7,000 within a short time. It struggled after its first few years; in 1963 it became a booklet-size biweekly and then folded altogether in 1966.

## 107-118.

## Little Friends Pictorial, 1959

Little Friends was one of the three best-selling children's magazines in the late 1950s, along with Children's Paradise and Children's Corner Pictorial Fortnightly. Its most popular columns included Panda Baby, Grandpa and Grandchild, Fanfan and Little Brother's Quiz, and The Story of Siu-keung. Each issue also featured a center spread drawn by Hui Guanman. Little Friends folded in 1992, after 33 years and more than 700 issues.

122.

# 7園楽小

![](images/a7b102bbc804ab374ddbedbb8db1a32e829feb94e3d425438cb2f3743f5c12c2.jpg)  
130.

![](images/5847e2b8b41cf12a205bfe58c37cd9412b3c863ae4d4e8c320a7112b3bd5a5ea.jpg)

# 園楽小 刊畫漫童兒智益

![](images/6b73ba5af7a0f9ee8a7c0647c425587494b0eff19b93ce8f8442f9eaaa52b925.jpg)  
133.  
132.

![](images/d35d1e7b9af492c83d6d67f6701c5353865dcf3ab2f6fea7cf21d3c156f7d473.jpg)

## 119–123.

Baby Comic, 1960

Baby Comic consisted of short stories and four-panel comic strips drawn by most of the manhua artists active at the time. Wong Chak's classic cartoon Master-Q (Lao Fuzi) was first published here; other stories included Caesar the Great, by Lee Sing-cheung, Tarzan, by Lee Kwok-yat, and Little Yellow Dragon, by Mak Ching.

## 124–132.

## Kid Paradise, 1961

This magazine was begun by a group of new artists, including Wong Chak, and Wu Shu-yu. Their freshness was a success, Kid Paradise was new and fashionable, and popular from the beginning. As the market's tastes changed, its style shifted from four-panel cartoon strips to longer stories. Like most manhua for children from this era, Kid Paradise’s visual style was greatly influenced by American cartoons, with round figures and smooth lines inspired by Disney.

## 133.

Children's Pictorial Story Series, 1961

Like most other children's magazines, this title published Western stories, and was usually half text and half pictures. The drawing style was heavily influenced by the West.

20

![](images/668684069b901d704c19facb2b6123dd07c9d3b38b83f7b7b37dbe697e98512a.jpg)

![](images/d4be4bd75f59993d07fc6e764d2af171c71e900fcd696cb70de7931024770bba.jpg)

![](images/b70e25db09c862bc8f2b5843e011f12f1927a9cc8d4598992644942aa68616cb.jpg)

![](images/6190a7a9707a1dfde7809ff4f2ee60992b5c05c038589bbecd2e7adb565e09e9.jpg)

![](images/b43d25861c9469b623464429b37c85b676ef61c8e18de7379cf3279dcbf6da5f.jpg)

![](images/077558688f0d36a585424cac51f82a2349953b61af471ab3448638d7d5d6ed52.jpg)

![](images/f68d46503dbee252fc41e98920156a8b1c59d571f6644e3e0119eb0d4bcce7f4.jpg)

![](images/6f4826a4ce50858de5bf0f2a775d39c6d7d068317b4cabe7847064a1d0e518e2.jpg)  
136.

![](images/c1a2df07f4b6d8782494ea775359cdaa0c706bb38c5895c04a60133a07b93e12.jpg)

歐洲童話一偷太陽的固王太陽光照耀着整個世界，使大家都能狗在明亮的白天生活和工作。但是故事中的国王竞收將屬於大家的太陽偷去，把太陽關在自己特梨的屋子裡，來醫治自己的烟瘡。世界漆黑一片，太陽价愁了，圆王的拥瘡不但没治好，反而被熾热的太陽烧成一堆炭灰，做壞事的人，常常會得到一個可恥的结果。

動人的故事一水上的孩子在艇上。他們多是以捕魚為生的漁民和载送人横。梁带强的一家就是窮苦的艇家他的爸爸出外工作未回，媽妈又病倒。他领进客進張保仔洞参觀；乞錢買電筒，都是想昧錢来给妈妈普病的。过說明了年杞小小的梁带强，就己經是一個非常懂事的好孩子。

蒙古民間傳說巴林样跤手摔跤是蒙古人喜愛的體育活動，與日本人的柔道有點相同。摔跤是以兩人互相徒手角力，能將對方摔倒在地，便算腾利，巴门從小刻苦级球自己，长大後便成病一個出色的摔政手。他代表巴林旗出賽·將烏珠旗的摔政手全部摔例。鳥珠旗王不是叫他的摔跋手吸收別人的長處·加紧訓練，提高拌跋技術；而是想盡辩法·要將獲得掉政冠軍的巴门殺害，迫得巴门離開自己的故鄉·逃到外地過达場災雅。請者們可以知道·乌珠旗王的確是一個不講道理的光狠的

小偵探故事答案：平時不發生這種情形的绿故，是因爲徐良真把指針住前一撞之後，它便起床上學了。以後間鐘再弊，他自然是不會聽到的。

新兒童之友

![](images/b6c61ce147b24a019b250adb081de0b19e8db81111ebd67aa83e4b04421df728.jpg)

## 134.

Youth Children's Story Series, 1961

The stories in this magazine came from both classic Chinese literature and Western stories. The target audience, students in the middle elementary grades, were treated to easy-to-read text, lively drawings, and positive messages. A typical story was Little Shui-hu Hero, a famous mythological tale (Outlaws of the Marsh) altered by making the main character a child.

135–138.

Children's Story, 1962

Originally a supplement to Little Friends Pictorial and geared to higher elementary and middle school children, this magazine was mainly text, but had some color illustrations. Features included The Story ofAhcheung, The Story of Little Detective Siu-fok, and The Story of Siu-yan.

139–141.

Little Angel Pictorial Monthly, 1962

Little Angel Pictorial Monthly was published by Children’'s Paradise and produced by the same writers and artists. Its contents were similar to Children's Paradise, but its illustrations were weak, and it included translated Western comic strips.

## 142.

Good Children's Pictorial Bimonthly, 1963

This was another imitator of Children's Paradise. It had very similar content, although it was targeted to Chinese children all over Southeast Asia, not just in Hong Kong. The wide distribution led to an interesting feature, a letters to the editor column full of notes from readers around the region.

## 143.

New Friends of Children Pictorial Bimonthly, 1965 Like the other children's magazines, this one had uplifting and positive themes running through its features, which included stories, science trivia, and games.

![](images/db16ee33d56733de3f551b68ad9cf91ea021aef2b4f25b966095622ab8e8cdeb.jpg)

156.

![](images/6bde704292df958d9dfbcbef0650a57359a75c6b3ddb25152e55a77e0ff0dc9e.jpg)

## 144-148.

## Little Baby and Little Po Po, 1975

Both of these comics were “candy books," a term used for inexpensive comics bought by young children. The characters were adorable, and the stories had a positive spin and educational content. Adults encouraged their children to read comics like Little Po Po and Little Baby,which is perhaps the reason they were published for almost twenty years. The art and scripts were by Hui Guan-man.

## 149–150. Children's TV, 1975

Most of this comic book's characters came from Japanese animation (anime) on television, which were a big hit in the early and mid-1970s. As this kind of programming continued to grow in popularity, the style of drawing and action began to influence Hong Kong manhua.

## 151-152.

## Happy Together Children's Pictorial, 1975

To appeal to the young fans of Japanese anime, Hong Kong manhua artists created stories using popular Japanese cartoon characters. This magazine's signature character is the round-faced TV Girly. Its stories are retellings of episodes of the Japanese show Robocon, which revolves around a red robot that can talk and work.

## 153–157.

## Ding Dong Bimonthly, 1976

Ding Dong, which first appeared in Children’s Paradise in 1975, is a translation and adaptation of the Japanese manga Doraemon, by Fuijo Fujiko. The main character, a nuclear-powered robot cat named Doraemon, became Ding Dong, and other places and character’s names were changed. The story is a heartwarming fantasy about Tai-hung, a little boy who is the master of Ding Dong. With Ding Dong's help, Tai-hung can fly (powered by a propeller on his head), fight evil classmates with special gadgets, and even travel through time via his desk drawer.

Readers of Children’'s Paradise loved Ding Dong, and it was soon published as its own comic book. However, it was an unauthorized copy of Doraemon, and copyright considerations led to its demise in 1981. Soon after, Dareaemon's Japanese publisher began printing an official Chinese version that preserved the pirated comic's character names. The title was finally changed to Doraemon in 1999, and it remains one of the most popular Japanese children's comic books in Hong Kong.

![](images/c9f9065be0fd32d162fa32a8d583f06bb4223cc6be9aab4fe4f652ba691297f6.jpg)

158.

![](images/1f34035ab0688c84a129e67b03fe95ca466b434c47cb7a42e51ba8663a0ff44f.jpg)

159.

![](images/09a55e3fb9cb9ba766546ea8ef729dbbba3268dbcf2c9577df6cb8d312350f74.jpg)  
160.  
162.

![](images/0c4d5542ae45b7a3d6d869c51a5c0c434951a0d17e7921f6c68822a89cf88d16.jpg)

## 158–162.

## Assorted pirated Japanese manga, 1977–79

Unauthorized reprints of Japanese manga, which sold for HK\$1, were very popular with children. The various titles—including Candy Candy, Ultraman, The Unbeatable Big Warrior Robot, and Robocon—targeted various age groups and both girls and boys. Most of these pirated comics did not credit the original artist or publisher.

## 163.

## Cute Girl, 1976

This short-lived picture magazine was named after a character in an ad for a Hong Kong noodle company. The two main characters, Cute Girl and Ox Boy, were based on traditional Chinese mythology. 'They act as “hosts” throughout the magazine. Features included The Dream of Cute Girl, The Story of Ox Boy and Weaver Girl, as well as a page for parents.

## 164–166.

## Big Brother Sunny, 1978

This illustrated magazine was inspired by the popular children’s TV show Tiu Fei-gei and revolves around its host, Big Brother Sunny. The program's young cast members also appear in it.

## 167–168.

## Sing Tao Good Children Happy Land, 1982

This addition to the Sing Tao Night Daily followed the format of its predecessor, the newspaper supplement introduced in 1957. Like the earlier version, it included Letters to Big Sister Lily and features such as Friend ofGood Children’s Activities, and Little Reporters, as well as illustrated stories. Its main manhua contributors were Lee Wai-chun and Ting Shui-leung.

## 169.

## Proverb Animation Gallery, 1987

These illustrated stories were based on a television program that explored Chinese proverbs. In each episode, a proverb was introduced, and its origin and meaning were discussed by the main characters, a panda scholar and a red robot named YY. After the series ended, the stories were collected into a bilingual book.

# 兒童快報 Whiz-kis:xpr ssWeekl

![](images/051b928869d0eefc79edc575195409a0f8c601b638f4af0fa7ea018b938d47b6.jpg)

176.

![](images/f5653184073739f5547305137bc00ed0e56a804a66965e0da82b11d73c1a6d16.jpg)

![](images/dfd7e5772f8201008a15794803d4c2c8d0315ce3923857ed60bc4761f6ca6955.jpg)

170.

## Children's Daily, 1989

Children’s Daily was the first tabloid-size daily newspaper for children. Its eight full-color pages included news, comics, and stories on sports, entertainment, science, and literature. The newspaper had great content in early issues and quickly achieved a circulation of 20,000 to 30,000. However, after a series of personnel changes, including the hiring of a new editor, the content and illustrations became less sophisticated, and the paper became less popular. It ceased publication in 1991.

## 171–173.

## Little Ming Pao Weekly, 1989

Since this publication was a supplement to Ming Pao Weekly, an entertainment magazine that came out every Sunday, it did not have to be as commercial as standalone comics. As a result, it tried out new artistic and editorial approaches, which were well received by its readers. Illustrators included recent design school graduates, who explored new techniques in their weekly stories. These young artists included Kwok Siuming, Mak Ga-bik, Siu Shek, and Wong Ging-tsai. The weekly’s most popular character was the adorable pig McMug, drawn by Mak Ga-bik with a script by Tse Lap-man. McMug later moved to the magazine Yellow Bus.

## 174–175.

## City Children's Weekly, 1990

The cover and contents of this weekly comic for children were drawn from Japanese manga. Appearances by the popular Hong Kong film character Shum Kinfan gave the title a celebrity boost. Each issue also included an educational supplement provided by a schoolbook publisher.

## 176–177.

## Whiz-Kids Express Weekly, 1993

This magazine began as a Sunday supplement to Express Daily and later became an individual magazine. The cover used Japanese manga to attract readers, although the contents included Hong Kong comics as well as educational stories.

182.

![](images/9d268fdbe0879ab1ac229792b145677829884c0da87a0a2d0f37b9bdddb9c1cf.jpg)

![](images/0589e9cadb70c3edfba440bf6cd456a4f04d701f2c7961503c3b78b06dcb361b.jpg)

![](images/510906a3f044b0536c6c5bd0340ef9c3d170703c24bf45474f57576372c88a42.jpg)

![](images/3bf992c44e306e5bdc0bd590c24ae56b0a53cd715127501e3dceeb8920d2cb90.jpg)

187.

178–189.

Yellow Bus, 1996

![](images/600289bed4393869f6d7bde8738458f4767a057e0101a035def7eec1050fb069.jpg)

Yellow Bus first appeared as a supplement to Sunday Weekly, but when that newspaper folded, the illustrated magazine's contents were adapted to a new audience. The new target audience was children accustomed to watching TV and playing video games, although the lively content and computer-generated illustrations also attracted young adult readers. Yellow Bus had other attractions, too. Readers could send in their photos and they would be morphed into fantastic images with animals or flowers. The magazine also often included stickers, badges, notebooks, erasers, and red packets for Chinese New Year.

![](images/39f9b6072403c2f14be7509a87eeebdf610ca032f47dcf7cf757a59001d4f49f.jpg)

![](images/56721abf332e95a0e2bf8006789f8cc6c25abbd7c777144aa62807f8cddebf08.jpg)

![](images/2b71552ec925f855f61e61c708a778f59029e7f5fcab1e627cfb657eb7fcbcfa.jpg)

![](images/7ddf1fef4c559cb3f6da33399893e9af9f70240cf512a96fc230be4d30c3af7f.jpg)

![](images/36e6ec6e7f677fb4148928eb6f3bd9297d70b0ddd3ca5308c509aa2d5833ffc0.jpg)

![](images/027f0ee79c30b7d475486b51f2190b468eaae0bef6b34b7c797c8cfa7524c204.jpg)

# reference sources

![](images/f193b6ed4e29b77e5a9cdb9578bf1c40970440559fea24f6c6788c8674fe1349.jpg)

![](images/c03f5ac3e15856d560382157a956a66c8fefe2da6b95ee9b54547b23e1b3d519.jpg)

![](images/c326e00f0c3aa2662e9e1b9943b90bdfa3504426a9876f3182c820a6984bc372.jpg)

How to Draw Cartoons, by Ngai Nam-shan, Nam-shan Publishing, 1960s. This is one of the earliest how-to books on drawing cartoons. Though primitive, it introduced the Hong Kong audience to basic cartoon drawing techniques.

Violence and Sex in Children's Comic Books, Hong Kong 1973–74, published by nongovernment social groups, 1974. This publication interviewed 918 elementary and middle school students on their reading habits and their opinions of comic books. It collected evidence of violence and sex from comic books and invited specialists to evaluate the influence of them on children.

The Cartoons Exhibition of Hong Kong, Hong Kong Arts Center, 1981. This is a catalog of an exhibition held at the Hong Kong Arts Center, jointly presented by Hong Kong Arts Center, the Cartoons Exhibition Organizing Committee, and the Consulate General of France. It includes an article by Cheng Ka-chun on “Forty Years of Manhua in Hong Kong and Guangzhou," Ah-wu's "The Common Language of Manhua People,"“Forty Years of Hong Kong Manhua,” “The Production of Animation Film in Hong Kong,” and “Meetings with Hong Kong Cartoon Artists, and Advertising Designers."

Cartoon Works Exhibition of Hong Kong, Cartoons Exhibition Organizing Committee, 1981. This booklet includes forty-three brief biographies of selected cartoon artists in Hong Kong from 1978 to 1980.

![](images/463351987dff59e571cf54efb990a1b0134e38941258258574e63328d669f3e5.jpg)

WOW! Wong Yuk-long, Hong Kong Cartoon Institute, 1984. This book is a detailed account of the life of Wong Yuk-long, beginning with his childhood, and includes a psychological and social analysis by specialists of the effects on readers of violence in comics.

![](images/79ae2697d17fe6734cf2e5cae721688d91e8f55d8f3fe585fea67e02bf8b58d1.jpg)

![](images/5db976c02eb3c447529c8c8c347bef2dd9e00b993c93b6d18f618ab4ef1e3090.jpg)

Selected Cartoons of Liao Bing-xiong, by Liao Bingxiong, edited by Yeung Wai-pong, Hong Kong Cartoon Institute, 1984. This collection covers thirty-nine important works of the artist from 1934 to 1984. They include Standard Slave (1930s), The Empire of Cart (1940s), Poet and Cartoon (1950s), Nightmare (late 1970s), Not Yet Sober (1984), and an artist biography.

The Selected Cartoons of Zheng Xin-yao, by Zheng Xinyao, Hong Kong Cartoon Institute, 1988. Zheng was the rising manhua artist in China in 1988. He was the art editor of New People Evening Post beginning in 1984, and his signature style is black humor.

![](images/9538422c23335121e254cfd8435b826fb10facba88131caedca01736f17d1558.jpg)

Seung-gun Siu-bo Autobiography, by Seung-gun Siubo, 1988. This autobiography records how the artist began working in the manhua industry, tells of the hardship of being a comic artist, and examines the history of the Hong Kong manhua industry.

![](images/e6115d9f6cd5acc802a86334c729683f23ae8a7463e90ddef1b3a63a40864df9.jpg)

Manhua News: The Retrospect of 50 Years of Cheng Kachun's Works, Hong Kong Cartoon Institute, 1988. This resource includes a brief history of Hong Kong comics by Cheng Ka-chun, as well as other articles by major manhua artists in the 1960s, such as Lam Cham, On Ping, Mak Ching, Au Ching, and Heung-shan Ah-wong.

![](images/8a6d5a8a08b93c5cdb60569a0ede8c0940dfc5042f58f5610a06c1cb9de9b1cb.jpg)

The Works of Ma Wing-shing, by Ma Wing-shing, 1989. This publication contains a brief biography of Ma, the reprint of the first issue of Tin-ha Pictorial, the making of Tin-ha Pictorial, and his new work as of 1989.

![](images/14e3f2e488ec8090a529e993a24f4a5d8ebbb6e4beaac4f05bf573f7495d6d94.jpg)

![](images/1b2a792b77abe8798e99f29a43701c3b813999a06db3254e90e8bf917a9819c8.jpg)

![](images/724b4ac5e5dcf3c798b2029ee0f9405fe536e51cdbfdabf777b41751c4037a95.jpg)

Contemporary Hong Kong Columnist Cartoon, Hong Kong Arts Center and Hong Kong Cartoon Institute, 1989. This exhibition catalog includes works from thirty-one participating cartoonists.

The Storms of Lianhuantu, by Lau Ting-kin, 1989. This book explores assorted topics about the lianhuantu industry. The contents lack organization and are a bit random.

Painting the Rainbow: The Biography ofMa Wing-shing, by Ma Wing-shing, Yau-woo Production Company, 1990. The book is about Ma's road to success and examines the author's life from childhood to adulthood,

![](images/bc3e46209feaf58b07c5ef347cb12ece583930209242e72b8166a3c06ec57a49.jpg)

資本漫畫論

![](images/27679f625ff512b4533b2965ab282b077f372030cd7abc8fef07927c46640055.jpg)

![](images/554e7faabc565129b334b35506deb364b325985577ff8435076c602fb8e5f1ed.jpg)

![](images/46afd41adb66ceeb874689b22e1ca8bbccb260d66674e73a69d0aa25d6e5c7a7.jpg)

The Chinese Comics Collection, by Pak-muk, Yau-woo Production Company, 1990. The book includes short articles on the development of comics in China, Hong Kong, ànd Taiwan, as well as a brief biography of Chinese comics artists.

![](images/e41f5707b7dff61716e812e583067c460522091cdc4565bcdd103e0addb4fe7f.jpg)

Manhua on Capitalism, by Lai Ba-lun, 1990. Lai Balun is a script writer for comics and writes of the Hong Kong manhua industry from an insider's perspective.

The Trends of Hong Kong Manhua, by Lai-ba-lun, 1990. The author introduces and analyzes the manhua industry in Hong Kong. The format of this book is uncommon.

Hong Kong Comic Artists 1990, Tin-ha Publishing, 1990. This book is one of the best biographical references on Hong Kong comics artists published in the 1990s. The detailed information makes it one of the most important sources on the history of Hong Kong comics.

![](images/5b658c70319ae211c6ebe14a4ab1e42ef45045ade3914ef7e1c982def7b2f6ce.jpg)

Manhua Classroom, by Ma Long, 1990. This book is a how-to about cartoons, but also includes a personal account of the author.

![](images/f66db1ce353de85a3c451fbda906aa78c649e1b7001cffddaaaa06c6e5affd75.jpg)

The Scandals of the Lianhuantau Industry, by Lau Dinggin, 1991. The book tells the untold story of the lianhuantau industry in Hong Kong in the 1970s and 1980s.

![](images/dfcaeb982ab7c8779159646ba80ecb561642ade52500eef64571b026c2f37802.jpg)

The Comics Are Bad, by To Mei-tse, 1991. The book is divided into four chapters: “Contemporary Lianhuantu," “Features on Selected Artists,”“Critics of Contemporary Lianhuantu," and “Talks on Lianhuantu." One section includes examples of selected classic lianhuantu.

![](images/b0e8ddb3a7dab7213db6813b6f9d583bd0adaf5cda1be7cfecda620390bcd829.jpg)

![](images/531d6a86050db130aca079f504cf50efbf0206a643ddbf460454db13c7de1b65.jpg)

The Language of Lianhuantu: The Birth of a Manhua, by Yuan Kin-toa, 1992. This book examines the visual language of comics and includes topics such as the dialogue balloon, frame, montage, camera angles, and the underlying meanings of various symbols.

The Records ofHong Kong Manhua, by Cheng Ka-chun, 1992. Cheng was one of the oldest manhua artist at the time this book was written. This is the first book on Hong Kong comics history, in an abbreviated format, with black and white illustration.

![](images/31e0631436359bac41be7326cd981f0da1b227a971eeac76f318906d122e0605.jpg)

The Critics of Wind and Cloud, by Ng Ka-keung, 1993. This book works as a critical analysis of the work of Wind and Cloud by Ma Wing-shing.

![](images/df80cdd2f9d4f4d12ed90ed6c83b38006ce38cd507878b7de1ef8fb55be25254.jpg)

Dream of Ten Billion, by Wong Yuk-long, 1994. Wong told the story of the development of his company from 1985 to 1991, including the legendary story of how his company became listed on the stock market, how he became a billionaire, his experience in high society, and how he lost his company.

![](images/55e8f2eff24022ee4f6081e5dc0837b72c98b358ac31af1fee30001e3c1aabbe.jpg)

Tiger in the Cage, by Wong Yuk-long, 1994. This book was written about the author's life, including his time in jail and what he learned from that experience.

![](images/9536355d9fc0010f599c4afe44a06efc935157cd141c452cafe0fc439e74a9ca.jpg)

The Good or the Bad: Textbook and Comic Book of Hong Kong (30s to 70s) Exhibition, Hong Kong Arts Center, 1994. This exhibition catalog records the classic examples of textbooks and comic books in Hong Kong from 1930 to the 1970s.

![](images/626cce11e19cb3c48b462f4ab478af73fc54b44057f8ead758389dc55128431a.jpg)

Comic in Revolt: The Shanghai Comics in the 30s and 40s, Hong Kong Arts Center, 1995. This exhibition catalog shows selected works with antiwar messages from comics magazines published in Shanghai during the 1930s and 1940s.

![](images/014238d1c1c97252231165a56de6af22d3232453465f7be06272b5a9847181a5.jpg)

Farewell Kiddy Cheung: A Tribute to the Hong Kong Cartoonist Yuan Bou-wan (1922–1995), Hong Kong Arts Center, 1996. Yuan belonged to the first generation of manhua artists born and raised in Hong Kong. He has been a household name since 1939, and this exhibition catalog records his life as a comic artist.

![](images/c0643a55540858e7fc0c7c62026fc0c44860ec71d3c26977bf3ce7117b66ba2c.jpg)

# selected artist biographies

## AU-YEUNG, Craig (1961–)

Au-Yeung Craig, often known simply as Craig, began drawing comics in 1981 while he was still in school. As a student, his work was published in Ming Pao’s Campus and Nonsense pages, and in Students’ Times Magazine. After receiving a degree in graphic design from Hong Kong Polytechnic in 1987, Au-Yeung worked as a disc jockey and art director for a radio station. His first graphic novel, Three Times Seven Equals Twenty-one, was published in late 1995. Since then, Au-Yeung has been a full-time comic artist; his work appears regularly in magazines and newspapers, and in comics such as Three Times Seven Equals Twentyone, My Own Private Heaven I to III, Siu Ming, and My Angel. In 1999 he established Cockroach Tenicolor Comix Quarterly, a quarterly venue for the work of young, alternative comics artists.

## BAO, Dik-cheung (1928–)

Known as Lo Fu, Hueng Chou, Uncle Seven, and Uncle Bao, Bao Dik-cheung came to Hong Kong around 1951, where he cofounded East Comics with Lee Waiman. Two years later, his popular comic Little Angeli, appeared; it was soon followed by other titles targeted at younger readers produced by Bao and his two brothers. Bao also served as editor of a children's cartoon supplement for Sing Tao Daily, and in the 1980s he briefly taught cartoon drawing at an art school.

## CHAN, Chi-dor ( ?)

Beginning in 1934, Chan's work was published in mainland newspapers. After World War II he moved to Hong Kong, where his comics, drawn under the pen name Uncle Luk, appeared in Sing Bao. He remained active in the comics industry for several decades; in the 1960s he was involved with the popular title Cartoons World.

## CHENG, Ka-chun (1918–2000)

Cheng Ka-chun published his first four-panel comic strips at the age of fifteen. During the Sino-Japanese war, Cheng, whose aliases included Cho Chi and Szeto Yan, was active in a Guangzhou antiwar artists group. After the war, he became the editor of Wah Kiu Daily and published Manhua World with Lee Fan-fu and other artists. An exhibition of his fifty-year manhua career was held at the Hong Kong Arts Center in 1988. Cheng also published a book on the history of Hong Kong manhua in 1992.

## CHEUNG, Man-yau (1957–)

Cheung Man-yau began working as an assistant to Wong Yuk-long at the age of seventeen. During his long association with the artist, he worked on several of Wong's popular comics, including Drunken Master and Buddha's Palm. Later Cheung became the chief artist on Oriental Heroes. He is known as a great draftsman, though his own works, such as Tournament and Warrior Horse, have not sold particularly well.

## CHOA, Yat (1968–)

Choa Yat, whose real name is Leung Chung-kei, was trained as a teacher and worked in that profession for three years before he returned to college for a bachelor’s degree in fine arts. His comics first appeared in the newspaper Ming Pao in 1991. Since then, his published comics have included Sam's Manhua Series and Leung's Saga Series.

## DONG, Pui-sun ( ?)

Dong Pui-sun, also known as Chou Si-wai, Chou Sun, and Fung Yan-king, worked as an illustrator for the World of Wu-hap in the 1960s before beginning to draw comics for major newspapers in the mid-1970s. His works include the Hai-dai Chau series in New Daily, The Nonsense in Tin Tin Daily, and Boss in Oriental Daily.

## FUNG, Chi-ming (1960–)

After joining the industry at age sixteen, Fung Chiming worked for various penny papers, including Hei Bo, Ching Bo, and Lianhuantu Daily. His best-selling work, Dagger, Sword, Laugh, was published in 1987. Recent works by Fung, include Enemy of Heaven, King of Dagger, and Tribe of X.

## HEUNG-SHAN, A-wong (1938–)

Heung-shan A-wong started his career in the 1950s in the mainland's manhua industry. Since he emigrated to Hong Kong in 1964, Heung-shan, whose real name is Wong Siu-tao, has been a regular contributor to Manhua World, Sing Tao Evening Daily, Oriental Daily, Ming Pao, Express Daily, and other newspapers and magazines. His best-known work includes Dummy Seven and Beauty, which first appeared in 1966.

## HON, Man (1949–)

Hon Man began working for the penny papers at the age of fifteen. In the early 1970s, Hon, whose real name is Cheung Chun-man, began to specialize in ghost stories. At Jademan Comics in the 1980s, he was responsible for titles such as High Spirit of Ghost and Strange Story Collection. He left Jademan in 1988 to publish his own comics, including Hon Man’'s Night Stories, then joined Kwong's Publishing, where he worked on Ghost World. He began to fade from the manhua industry after that and worked as fortune-teller.

## HuI, Guan-man (1930–)

Hui Guan-man started to submit his work to newspapers in the early 1950s and quickly became one of Hong Kong's most popular and well-paid manhua artists. His works include Uncle Choi, Joke's Comics, Grandpa and Granddaughter, Young Couple, Miss Silly, and Handsome Duck. Hui retired in the 1980s.

## HuI, King-sam (?)

Hui King-sam first worked as an assistant on Ma Wingshing's The Chinese Hero. His own comic, The King ofStreet Fighter, became one of Jademan Comics’s bestsellers and spawned several imitators.

## KAM, Siu-man (1962–)

Kam Kin-man, known as Kam Siu-man, joined the comics industry at age sixteen as an assistant and later joined Jademan Comics. In the mid-1980s he gained acclaim for Tai-kung Pao, which appeared in Yuk-long Manhua Biweekly. After leaving Jademan, Kam went to Siu-keung Publishing, where he worked on Siu-man Classic Four Frames Works. Since 1997 his manhua strips have appeared mainly in newspapers and magazines.

## KHOO, Fuk-lung (1964–)

Khoo Fuk-lung joined Wong's Jademan Comics in 1980, where he worked on Siu-keung Manhua Collection, and Yuk-long Manhua Biweekly and drew covers for several other comics. His first individual work, Steel Warrior Adventures, appeared in 1989. In 1993 Khoo redrew Wong's famous work Solar Lord, which had been popular in the 1970s. By then, Khoo had become well-known for his own fighting comics.

## LAI, Tat-wing (1971–)

Lai Tat-wing, also known as Tat-tat-wing, first published his comics in Sau-sing Chai (Longevity Boy) when he was still in junior high school. His work was later used for theater-company publicity materials, and his short stories appeared in several magazines. In 1995 his first self-published work, Man-man Chu, Dak-dak Gao, received a positive response from alternativecomics readers. Since then he has drawn several other titles in the spirit of Man-man, and his works have developed a more mainstream audience.

## LAU, Lee-lee (1966–)

After graduating from Hong Kong Polytechnic with a degree in graphic design, Lau Lee-lee worked for a nongovernmental organization for five years. She began to draw comics for nonmainstream magazines and in 1998 became a full-time comics artist. Her first collection, Mom's Drawer Is at the Bottom, spurred discussions about feminism and gender-equality issues. Lau has since published comics in several major newspapers and contributed to Au-Yeung Craig’s Cockroach Tenicolor Comix Quarterly. Other titles by Lau include This Is How Star Should Really Be, Two Sunny Side Up, Tossing and Turning, The Beginning of the End, and Flat World.

## LAW, Guan-tsiu (1918–)

Law Guan-tsiu, who received formal art training in Guangzhou, came to Hong Kong after World War II. In 1953, he established the first full-color children's magazine in Hong Kong, Children's Paradise, which he worked on for thirty years. After retiring from the magazine, he started a studio, where he taught drawing classes. In the mid-1990s, Law was elected the visual artists representative to the Hong Kong Arts Development Council.

## LEE, Chi-ching (1963–)

Lee Chi-ching joined the penny paper Ching Bo in 1981 and later worked for Jademan Comics's Manhua King Weekly and Ghost Stories Collection. After Khoo Fuklung left Jademan, Lee worked on Khoo's Steel Warrior Adventures. Lee's most recognized work, The Story of Three Countries, was the first Hong Kong manhua to sell well in Japan. His most recent works are manhua versions of Jing Yong's epic sword-fighting novels.

## LEE, Dak-hong (1957–)

Lee Dak-hong began publishing comic strips in Hong Kong's major daily papers and magazines in 1980. His individual works include Manhua by Lee Dak-hong and Little Funny Booklet.

## LEE, Fan-fu (1906–1967)

The career of Lee Fan-fu, whose real name was Lee Tak-chun, began in Guangzhou in the late 1920s. In 1929, he worked with Ye Yin-quan to organize that city’s first comics magazine, The Sketch. Lee's most famous works from this period are Young Master and Old Master Ho. After World War II ended, Lee moved to Hong Kong, where he continued to draw Young Master; it was published in Sing Bao until he died. He also contributed to Manhua World, one of the most important comics in Hong Kong.

## LEE, Ling-hon (1928–)

Lee Ling-hon, whose real name is Lee King-wah, began to publish his work at the age of seventeen in mainland China. He continued to draw comics after moving to Hong Kong in 1949. Lee contributed to various newspapers through the 1950s and 1960s, as well as to such manhua publications as Caricature Weekly and Man-hua Daily. Lee moved to Canada in 1982.

## LEE, Shek-cheung (1928–)

Lee Shek-cheung came to Hong Kong in 1946 and began working for New Children's Biweekly Magazine. In 1959 he helped found Hong Kong's longestrunning children’s magazine, Little Friends Pictorial. A few years later, his four-panel strip Dai-wah and Siuwah began to appear regularly in a pro-China newspaper. That strip has been in print for almost twenty years.

## LEE, Wai-chun (?)

Lee Wai-chun is one of the very few well-established female manhua artists. Her first work, Miss Fa Fa (Miss Flower), was not a big hit, but it did lead to her next work, 13-Dot Cartoons, which was first published in July 1969. 13-Døt was very popular with female readers and remained in print until 1980. In July 1996, 13- Dot was republished as a “cartoon classic” by the biggest publisher in Hong Kong, Publication (Holdings) Limited. This version ceased in 1999.

## Li, Chi-tat (1965–)

Li Chi-tat has worked in the manhua industry since he was seventeen. He is best known for his realistic drawing style, which resembles the work of the Japanese manga artist Katsuhiro Otomo. Lee's works include Black Mask and The Assassination of Emperor Qin.

## LIAO, Bing-xiong (1915–)

Born in Guangzhou, Liao Bing-xiong went to a teacher’s college, then worked as a teacher and an art editor before starting to draw comics. During the Sino-Japanese War, Liao specialized in antiwar manhua; after the war he was a member of Renjian Huahui, a Chinese comics artists orgànization in Hong Kong. He returned to China in the early 1950s, and there he became one of the mainland's most important manhua artists.

## LUI, Yu-tin (1927–)

Lui Yu-tin, who was born in Hong Kong, drew comics for various Hong Kong and Guangzhou newspapers. His most famous work, Wu-lung Wong (King of Blunders), first appeared in Chum Daily in Guangzhou. After the war Wu-lung Wong became the most popular comic series in southern China and Hong Kong, and it inspired several movies during the 1950s and 1960s. Lui moved to Canada in the 1980s.

## MA, Long (1952–)

Ma Long, whose real name is Yuan Jao-chiu, began working in the comics business in 1983. His first comics appeared in Sun Pao, Ming Pao, and Sing Tao Daily. Ma’s individual works include Fifty Steps, Ma Long’s Political Cartoon Collection, The Transition Period 1991–1992 Manhua Collection, and Humorous Cartoons by Ma Long. In 1991 he worked as an editor for Fan Dou Magazine, and in 1998 he edited Special Administrative Region Manhua Magzine, funded by the Hong Kong Arts Development Council.

## MA, Wing-shing (1961–)

Ma Wing-shing began his career at the age of fifteen at Ching Bo, where his comics included The Experiment of the Devil, Five Brothers, and Charming. In the 1980s Ma joined Jademan Comics and created The Chinese Hero, which became the all-time best-selling comic in Hong Kong. In 1989 he started his own company and published Tin Ha Pictorial, which featured two of his comics, The North and South Poles and Wind and Cloud. Ma has also worked in collaboration with other artists to create Black Leopard (with Siu Kit), God of Kings (with Yuan Ga-bo), and Enemy of Heaven (with Fung Chi-ming). Both The Chinese Hero and Wind and Cloud were made into movies.

## MAK, Ching (1934–)

Mak Ching migrated from Macau to Hong Kong, where he served as the editor of Manhua World from 1957 to 1965. He continued to be a productive artist through the 1970s, publishing his work in Caricature Weekly, Manhua Daily, and Baby Comic.

## NG, Sheng-gwan (1935–)

Ng Sheng-gwan's work first appeared in Kung-sheng Daily in the 1950s, and later in Express Daily and the Sun Dan Evening Post. His comics include Careless Young Master, Happy Fruit, and Big Brother Sunny.

## OW, Man (1960–)

Ow Man, whose real name is Man Kai-ming, began working in the manhua industry at the age of fourteen and has served under both Seung-gun Siu-bo and Wong Yuk-long. At Wong's Jademan Comics in the 1980s, he was responsible for the drafting work on Buddha's Palm. In 1993 Ow formed his own company, King's Fountain Limited, and published several gangster manhua set in Hong Kong’s red light district, including Teddy Boy and Portland Street. Teddy Boy was made into a movie in 1996; four sequels followed.

## SEUNG-GUN, Siu-bo (1955–)

Seung-gun Siu-bo, born Kwong Tong-yuen, entered the manhua industry at age 11. His early works, drawn in the late 1960s, include Unbeatable Golden Dagger, Black and White Little Demon, Little Drunken God, and Little Bar Girl. Seung-gun's most successful comic, the kung fu manhua Lee Siu-lung (Bruce Lee), debuted in 1972. The manhua empire he built, which included the penny papers Hei Bo and Ching Bo, made him Wong Yuk-long's major competitor throughout the 1970s. Wong convinced him to work at Jademan Comics in the 1980s, though that arrangement lasted only a short while. In 1986 Seung-gun established Kwong's Publishing, where he has continued to publish Lee Siu-lung and other kung fu manhua.

## SEUNG-GUN, Siu-keung (1950–)

Chan Kwok-yin, who publishes under the name Seunggun Siu-keung, has worked in the comics industry since he was sixteen. In 1973 he began to work on 73- Manhua, which published his strip Sau-sing Chai (Longevity Boy). The following year he joined Jademan Comics and continued the title. In 1988 Seung-gun left Jademan to start his own company; its titles include Sau-sing Chai and Siu-keung Manhua Collection.

## SEUNG-GUN, Siu-wai (1950–)

Seung-gun Siu-wai, whose real name is Kwong Namlum, is Seung-gun Siu-bo’s older brother. He started working in the manhua industry in 1963, when he was thirteen. Ten years later, he started the magazine 73-Manhua, and two years after that he began collaborating with his brother on Hei Bo. Like his brother, Seung-gun joined Jademan Comics in the 1980s, where he contributed to King Manhua Weekly. After leaving Jademan, he formed his own company.

## SIU, Kit (1963–)

At the age of fifteen, Siu Kit assisted Ma Wing-shing in the production of Charming. He continued to work with Ma at Jademan Comics and later became the first assistant on Ma’s The Chinese Hero. When Ma Wingshing established his own company in 1989, Siu once again worked as his key assistant, this time for Tin Ha Pictorial's North and South Poles and Wind and Cloud. Siu's other joint works with Ma include Black Leopard.

## SUNG, Sam-long (1926–1999)

Sung Sam-long's work first appeared in Manhua World in the 1950s. His most famous comic is Dumb Detective Manhua Series; he also worked as an art editor for various newspapers during the 1970s and 1980s.

## TOU, Sam (1950–)

Tou Sam's comics have been appearing since 1975 in such venues as Sing Tao Night Daily, Oriental Daily, City Magazine, Children's Daily, and Ming Pao. His best-known titles include One Is Nice and Three Little Guys.

## WAN, Yat-leung (1967–)

Wan Yat-leung, alias Fat Leung, joined the industry at age nineteen as an assistant at Kwong's Publishing. After working as a producer and script writer on King of Ghost Book II: If I Am the King, he left Kwong's to self-publish End of the World, which lasted only a few issues. His most popular works include The Legend of Black Panther and Sea Tiger I–III.

## WONG, Chak (1922–)

Wong Chak, whose real name is Wong Kai-hei, moved to Hong Kong in the 1950s to work as an illustrator for a Catholic magazine. In the late 1950s he began submitting his work to Manhua World and the comics page of Sing Tao Daily. Shortly after that, he created the manhua characters Old Master Q, Big Dumb, and Mr. Chun, who appeared in Wong's most successful title, Old Master Q. This comic has been in print since 1964, which makes Old Master Q Hong Kong's longestliving manhua character.

## WONG, Kwok-hing ( ?)

Wong Kwok-hing joined the industry in 1976 as an assistant to Seung-gun Siu-bo. His first individual work was a kung fu comic titled Grasshopper Boy. Wong also contributed to Yuk-long Manhua Biweekly and in 1988 published Comic Pig.

## WONG, Sze-ma (1940– 1983)

Wong Sze-ma, born Wong Wing-hing, started drawing newspaper comic strips in 1961. Four years later he began publishing the four-panel strip Father and Ms. in the newspaper Ming Pao. After Wong died in 1983, Publication (Holdings) Ltd. released collections of his major works, including Ngau-chai, Gorgeous Susan and Debussy.

## WONG, Yuk-long (1950–)

Wong Yuk-long, known as Tony Wong in English, was the major force in Hong Kong manhua during the 1980s and 1990s. Wong started his comics career at age thirteen at Epoch Comic Weekly. By the time he was twenty-one, he had already tried to start his own company four times. He succeeded in 1971, establishing Yuk-long Picture Book Company; his works from this period include Little Vagabond, The Son of Ultraman, and Solar Lord. The next year saw the debut of his best-selling kung fu manhua Little Rascals (Siu Lau-man), later renamed Oriental Heroes (Lung Fu Mun). Wong also established two penny papers, Sang Po and Golden Bo Daily, in 1975 and 1977, respectively. About this time he set up a formal system for hiring assistants, which helped the manhua industry become more organized and more like other media industries. In the 1980s Wong established Jademan Comics and began to consolidate the industry by hiring his main competitors, including Seung-gun Siubo and Seung-gun Siu-wai. Jademan eventually had a virtual monopoly on the Hong Kong comics industry In July 1986 Jademan (Holdings) Limited was listed ón the Hong Kong stock market—it was the peak of Wong's career. In October of 1987, the stock market crashed and brought his company down along with it. He was pushed out of Jademan, and in 1991 he was sent to prison for two years for forgery. After his release, Wong established Jade Dynasty and began publishing comics again.

## YEN, E-king (1933–)

Yen E-king, alias Old Monster or Ah-chun, never had a fixed drawing style. His best-known works are the editorial cartoons on social and political issues that he drew in the 1960s for Express Daily. In 1968 these were collected and published as The World ofE-king Yen. In the 1990s, under the alias Ah-chun, he published a series of comics done in the style of traditional Chinese paintings.

## YUAN, Bou-wan (1922–1995)

Yuan Bou-wan, whose real name was Yuan Chi-tang, began publishing his work in Guangzhou newspapers before World War II. The southern Chinese drawing style he picked up there later influenced Hong Kong manhua. Yuan's most famous characters include Kiddy Cheung, Miss Lau, Hot-Tempered Leung, Arrogant Chiu, and Wong Chai. Kiddy Cheung and Miss Lau were the most popular comics, and were made into movies during the 1950s.

## YUAN, Yat-mok (1957–)

In 1975 Yuan Jao-tai, known as Yuan Yat-mok, Yatmo, and Local Boy, helped organize an experimental comic daily and later drew comics for newspaper and magazine columns. Most of his work has criticized politics and current events. Yuan's individual works include Rather Die Than Change.

## ZUNZı (1955–)

Zunzi, whose real name is Wong Kei-kwan, began drawing comics in 1980. He has had regular editorial cartoon columns in Ming Pao, People’s Monthly and Next Magazine, and Apple Daily and has published stand-alone comics such as Black Material, Grandpa Dang, 100 Caricatures of Hong Kong, Taiwan, and China Political Figures, and A Biography of Mr. Legislator.
